Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Introduction to Adab al-Bahth and Logical Preliminaries
Front matter showing book title, author, publisher details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and scope of the classical work on dialectical debate (munazara) from its front matter.

Core Idea
Front matter reveals the book's purpose: logical tools for structured Islamic debate.

This lesson orients you to the work 'Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara' by Muhammad al-Amin al-Shanqiti. Though the OCR is degraded, we can extract key information: the book is divided into sections, with this first part covering logical preliminaries essential for formal debate in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the book title and author
Find the title phrase 'المناظرة' (munazara) and the author name 'محمد الأمين بن محمد المختار الجكني الشنقيطي'. This tells you the subject (dialectical debate) and the scholarly lineage of the author.
OCR corruption rendered the title as 'الراب الحية المناظرة', but the intended name is likely 'آداب البحث والمناظرة'.
2
Identify the series and publisher
Notice the mention of 'نظمة التعاون الاسلامي' (Organization of Islamic Cooperation) and 'دار عطاءات العلم' and 'دار ابن حزم'. This indicates the book is part of an institutional publication series, adding authority.
3
Note the section heading
The phrase 'القسم الأول مقدمات منطقية' means 'First Section: Logical Preliminaries'. This tells you that before entering debate proper, the reader must master logical foundations.
This confirms the lesson type: memorization anchor for foundational concepts.
4
Understand the discipline: munazara
Munazara is a formal discipline of structured argumentation in Islam, distinct from general logic. It has its own rules for proposing, objecting, and replying, used in fiqh, kalam, and usul.
This key term will recur throughout the course.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نظمة التعاون الاسلامي الدولي الفقه الإسلامي مطبوعات المجتمع الراب الحية المناظرة تأليف الشيخ العلامة محمد الأمين بن محمد المختار الجكني الشنقيطي ۱۳۹۲ - ٢٥ ١٣ تحقيق سعود بن عبد العزيز العريفي إشراف د بن عبد اللاهورنك القسم الأول مقدمات منطقية دار عطاءات العلم دار ابن حزم
Organization of Islamic Cooperation, International Islamic Fiqh, Publications of the Society, The Living League of Debate, authored by the eminent scholar Muhammad al-Amin bin Muhammad al-Mukhtar al-Jakani al-Shanqiti, 1392 - 25 13, edited by Sa'ud bin Abd al-Aziz al-Uraifi, supervised by Dr. Bin Abd Allah Warnak, First Section: Logical Preliminaries, Dar Ata'at al-Ilm, Dar Ibn Hazm.
Source pages p. 1

Commentary
This passage is the title page and publication information of the book. It identifies the work as part of a series by the Organization of Islamic Cooperation, focusing on Islamic Fiqh. The author is the renowned scholar Muhammad al-Amin al-Shanqiti. The book is titled 'The Living League of Debate' as rendered by OCR, but probably refers to 'Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara' (The Etiquette of Research and Debate). The page indicates that this volume is the first section dealing with logical preliminaries. The publication is joint by Dar Ata'at al-Ilm and Dar Ibn Hazm, with the fifth edition in 1441 AH/2019 CE.

Checkpoint
What is the main subject of this book based on the front matter?
A) Islamic Fiqh rulings
B) The etiquette of debate and logical preliminaries
C) The life of the author
D) International cooperation

Key Takeaway
Front matter establishes the book's subject as the logical foundations necessary for structured Islamic debate, authored by a respected scholar and published under scholarly supervision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the front matter of another Islamic logical work (e.g., al-Risala al-Shamsiyya) and identify the author, subject, and section division. Write one sentence summarizing the book's scope.
?
Discuss with a peer: why do you think the author placed 'Logical Preliminaries' before the actual rules of debate? What might a debater miss if they skipped this section?

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Why a Da'i Must Learn Logic
Author explains the importance of learning logic to counter sophistical arguments in da'wah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why learning logic is necessary for da'wah and identify the defining characteristic of sophistry.

Core Idea
Logic is the weapon of a da'i against sophistical deception.

The author sets the stage for the entire book by highlighting a critical challenge facing Muslim callers: they will encounter opponents who use deceptive arguments to twist truth into falsehood. To fulfil the duty of da'wah effectively, Muslims must arm themselves with the knowledge that can dismantle such sophistry. This lesson unpacks that argument and defines the core enemy: sophistry itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the passage and pinpoint why the author says Muslims must learn a special knowledge. The problem is that callers to falsehood use 'philosophical fallacies and sophistical premises' to confuse people.
Sophistry is not just lying; it uses clever but false reasoning.
2
Understand the effect of sophistry
The author says these deceptive arguments 'present truth in the form of falsehood and falsehood in the form of truth' and 'silence many students of knowledge.' Reflect on why this is especially dangerous for da'wah.
If truth appears false, people may reject it; if falsehood appears true, they may embrace it.
3
Grasp the solution
The author asserts that Muslims must learn 'that knowledge by which they can invalidate those arguments.' This knowledge is logic (ʿilm al-manṭiq), the subject of the book.
The author views logic as a defensive tool, not just an abstract discipline.
4
Define sophistry
Look at the footnote (1): sophistry is defined as 'a syllogism composed of illusory premises (wahmiyyāt), the purpose of which is to mislead the opponent and silence him.' Identify the two key components: the material (illusory premises) and the goal (mislead and silence).
Al-Jurjānī's definition focuses on the deceptive nature and the intent to overpower the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
الحمد لله رب العالمين، وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، وأشهد أن محمدًا عبده ورسوله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وصحبه وسلم تسليمًا.
أما بعد:
فإنه لما كان من أهداف الجامعة الإسلامية نشر العلم، وتعليم أبناء المسلمين بصفة عامة وتخريج الدعاة إلى الله بصفة خاصة، وكانت الدعوة إلى الله من أهم أعمال المسلمين جميعًا، فإن الجامعة مازالت دائبة في تدعيم مناهجها إلى أن أصبحت ـ ولله الحمد ـ في المستوى المنهجي اللائق بها وبأهدافها.
وقد تخرّج أفواج من أبنائها من كلية الشريعة، شغلوا مناصب عدةً من تدريس وقضاء ودعوة.
ولما كان من المتوقع أن يواجه الدعاة إلى الحق دعاة إلى الباطل مضللون، يجادلون لشبه فلسفية ومقدمات سوفسطائية (١)، وكانوا لشدة تمرنهم على تلك الحجج الباطلة كثيرًا ما يُظهرون الحق في صورة الباطل والباطل في صورة الحق ويفحمون كثيرًا من طلبة العلم الذين لم يكن معهم سلاح من العلم يدفع باطلهم بالحق، وكان من الواجب على المسلمين أن يتعلموا من العلم ما يتسنّى لهم به إبطال تلك الحجج.
(١) (السفسطة : قياس مركب من الوهميّات، والغرض منه تغليط الخصم وإسكاته). التعريفات للجرجاني : ص ١٢٤ .
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and I bear witness that there is no god but Allah alone, no partner with Him, and I bear witness that Muhammad is His servant and Messenger, may Allah bless him and his family and his Companions and grant them peace. To proceed: Since among the objectives of the Islamic University is spreading knowledge, educating the children of Muslims in general, and graduating callers to Allah in particular, and since calling to Allah is among the most important deeds of all Muslims, the university has continued to strengthen its curricula until it has reached – praise be to Allah – the appropriate methodological level befitting it and its objectives. Groups of its graduates from the College of Shari'a have graduated and occupied various positions in teaching, judiciary, and da'wah. And since it was expected that the callers to truth would face misleading callers to falsehood, who argue using philosophical fallacies and sophistical premises (1), and due to their intense practice of those false arguments, they often present truth in the form of falsehood and falsehood in the form of truth, and they silence many students of knowledge who did not have the weapon of knowledge to refute their falsehood with truth, it became incumbent upon Muslims to learn that knowledge by which they can invalidate those arguments. (1) Sophistry: a syllogism composed of illusory premises, the purpose of which is to mislead the opponent and silence him. al-Ta'rifat by al-Jurjani, p. 124.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is an introductory statement from the book 'Adab Bahth' (The Discipline of Discussion), setting the context and motivation for the study of logic. The author begins with the basmala and hamdalah, then explains the role of the Islamic University in spreading knowledge and graduating da'is (callers to Islam). He notes that many graduates have taken up positions in teaching, judiciary, and da'wah. The central point is that da'is will inevitably encounter false propagandists who use philosophical fallacies and sophistical arguments. These sophists are so skilled at deception that they make truth appear false and falsehood appear true, often overwhelming students who lack the intellectual weapons to counter them. Therefore, the author asserts, it is obligatory for Muslims to learn the type of knowledge that enables them to invalidate such arguments – this knowledge is logic ('ilm al-mantiq). The footnote defines sophistry as a syllogism composed of illusory premises meant to mislead and silence the opponent, citing al-Jurjani's definition. The passage thus functions as a justification for the entire subject of the book: arming the Muslim da'i with the logical tools to defend the truth against sophisticated falsehood.

Key Terms
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the introduction, what is the primary reason Muslims must learn logic (or the type of knowledge mentioned)?
A) To excel in academic debates at the university.
B) To refute the deceptive arguments of false propagandists who use sophistry.
C) To fulfill a requirement for graduation from the College of Shari'a.
D) To understand philosophical writings more deeply.

Key Takeaway
Sophistry is a deliberate deceptive syllogism using illusory premises; logic is the essential tool for a da'i to defend the truth against such distortion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Describe a scenario where a sophist might use an illusory premise (wahmi) to confuse a listener about the obligation of prayer. Then explain how knowing the definition of sophistry would help a da'i respond.
?
Task 2: The author says sophistry 'presents truth in the form of falsehood and falsehood in the form of truth.' Give a concrete example from Islamic history (e.g., arguments against the prophethood of Muhammad) and explain how logical analysis would uncover the deception.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Introduction to Adab al-Bahth: Purpose and Necessity
Author explains why the university introduced Adab al-Bahth to counter sophistry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Islamic University introduced Adab al-Bahth and how it equips callers to counter sophistry using logical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Adab al-Bahth is the shield of the caller against sophistical arguments.

This passage opens a textbook on Adab al-Bahth by explaining why the Islamic University added this subject to the College of Da'wah curriculum. The author describes the threat of sophisticated sophists who twist truth into falsehood and vice versa. To fulfill the duty of da'wah, callers must be armed with a science that can identify logical errors and present convincing evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the university's mission
Find the stated goals of the Islamic University in the opening lines. What is its primary focus?
2
Understand the challenge posed by sophistry
How does the author describe the opponents callers will face? What tactics do they use?
Sophistry is defined in the footnote as a syllogism built on imaginary premises.
3
Pinpoint the solution proposed
What action did the university take to prepare callers? Which new college was established and what subject was introduced?
4
See the logical foundation
Why does the author insist that understanding logic is necessary for mastering Adab al-Bahth? What elements of logical syllogisms are mentioned?
The author refers to terms like 'minor premise', 'major premise', and 'middle term'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سم الله الرحمن الرحيم
الحمد لله رب العالمين، وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، وأشهد أن محمدًا عبده ورسوله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وصحبه وسلم أجمعين .
أما بعد :
فإنه لما كان من أهداف الجامعة الإسلامية نشر العلم، وتعليم أبناء المسلمين بصفة عامة وتخريج الدعاة إلى الله بصفة خاصة، وكانت الدعوة إلى الله من أهم أعمال المسلمين جميعًا، فإن الجامعة مازالت دائبة في تدعيم مناهجها إلى أن أصبحت ـ ولله الحمد ـ في المستوى المنهجي اللائق بها وبأهدافها .
وقد تخرّج أفواج من أبنائها من كلية الشريعة، شغلوا مناصب عدةً من تدريس وقضاء ودعوة.
ولما كان من المتوقع أن يواجه الدعاة إلى الحق دعاة إلى الباطل مضللون، يجادلون لشبه فلسفية ومقدمات سوفسطائية (١)، وكانوا لشدة تمرنهم على تلك الحجج الباطلة كثيرًا ما يُظهرون الحق في صورة الباطل والباطل في صورة الحق ويفحمون كثيرًا من طلبة العلم الذين لم يكن معهم سلاح من العلم يدفع باطلهم بالحق، وكان من الواجب على المسلمين أن يتعلموا من العلم ما يتسنّى لهم به إبطال الباطل وإحقاق الحق على الطرق المتعارفة عند عامة الناس = حمل ذلك الجامعة على إنشاء كلية الدعوة وأصول الدين.
ومهمتها تخريج دعاة قادرين على تبليغ الدعوة بالحكمة والموعظة الحسنة، وعلى إفحام وإلزام الدعاة المضللين، ببيان ما يصحح أدلتهم ، ويظهر بطلان حجج خصومهم .
ومن أجل ذلك قررت في منهج هذه الكلية تدريس مادة آداب البحث والمناظرة ؛ لأنه هو العلم الذي يقدر به من العلم الذي يقدر به من تعلمه علی بیان الغلط مواضع في حُجة خصمه، وعلى تصحيح مذهبه بإقامة الدليل المقنع على صحته، أو صحة ملزومه، أو بطلان نقيضه، ونحو ذلك . ومن المعلوم أن المقدمات التي تتركب منها الأدلة التي يحتج بها كل واحد من المتناظرين إنما تُوجه الحجة بها منتظمة على صورة القياس المنطقي .
ومن أجل ذلك كان فن آداب البحث والمناظرة» يتوقف فهمه كما ينبغي على فهم ما لابد منه من فن المنطق؛ لأن توجيه السائل المنع على المقدمة الصغرى أو الكبرى مثلاً ، أو القدح في الدليل بعدم تكرار الحد الوسط، أو باختلال شرط من شروط الإنتاج ونحو ذلك(١) ، لا يفهمه من لا إلمام له بفن المنطق .
وكانت الجامعة قد أسندت إلينا تدريس فن آداب البحث (١) سيأتي بيان هذه المصطلحات عند الكلام على القياس المنطقي وأركانه ص ١٠٦ وما بعدها .
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and I bear witness that there is no deity except Allah alone, with no partner, and I bear witness that Muhammad is His servant and Messenger, may Allah bless him and his family and all his Companions. To proceed: Since among the goals of the Islamic University are spreading knowledge, educating Muslim children in general, and graduating callers to Allah in particular, and since calling to Allah is among the most important works of all Muslims, the university has continued to strengthen its curricula until it has reached — praise be to Allah — the methodological level befitting it and its goals. And groups of its graduates from the College of Shari'ah have occupied various positions of teaching, judiciary, and da'wah. And since it was expected that the callers to truth would face misleading callers to falsehood who argue with philosophical doubts and sophistical premises (1), and due to their intense practice of those false arguments, they often present truth in the form of falsehood and falsehood in the form of truth, and they overwhelm many students of knowledge who have no weapon of knowledge to repel their falsehood with truth, and it was obligatory upon Muslims to learn from knowledge what enables them to invalidate falsehood and establish truth by the methods common among the general public — this prompted the university to establish the College of Da'wah and Usul al-Din. Its mission is to graduate callers capable of conveying the call with wisdom and good exhortation, and of silencing and compelling the misleading callers, by clarifying what corrects their evidence and exposes the invalidity of their opponents' arguments. For that reason, the curriculum of this college decided to teach the subject of Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara (the art of discussion and debate); because it is the science by which the one who learns it is able to identify the places of error in his opponent's argument, and to correct his own doctrine by presenting convincing evidence for its correctness, or the correctness of its implication, or the invalidity of its contradiction, and so on. And it is known that the premises composing the evidence that each of the two disputants employs are only directed as arguments when they are systematically arranged in the form of a logical syllogism. Therefore, the proper understanding of the art of Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara depends on understanding what is necessary from the art of logic; because directing the questioner's objection to the minor or major premise, for example, or attacking the evidence due to the non-repetition of the middle term, or the violation of a condition of productivity and the like (1) — this is not understood by one who has no familiarity with the art of logic. And the university had entrusted us with teaching the art of Adab al-Bahth (1) The explanation of these terms will come in the discussion on the logical syllogism and its pillars on page 106 and beyond.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is the preface (front matter) of a textbook on Adab al-Bahth. It begins with the basmalah and hamdalah, then explains the rationale for introducing this subject in the Islamic University's College of Da'wah. The author states that the university aims to spread knowledge and train callers to Allah. Since callers to truth will face sophisticated opponents who use philosophical doubts and sophistical arguments (sophistry defined in a footnote as a syllogism composed of imaginative premises intended to deceive and silence the opponent), it is necessary to equip students with the tools to refute falsehood and establish truth. Therefore, the university established the College of Da'wah and Usul al-Din to graduate callers capable of wise and effective argumentation. The specific subject, Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara, is described as the science that enables one to identify errors in an opponent's argument and to correct one's own position by providing convincing evidence. The author emphasizes that the arguments in debates are structured as logical syllogisms (qiyas). Thus, understanding logic is essential for properly applying the rules of debate, such as objecting to a premise (minor or major) or criticizing the lack of repetition of the middle term. The passage concludes by noting that the university assigned the author to teach this subject, with a footnote referring to later explanations of these terms.

السفسطة
القياس المنطقي

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Why Logic is Essential for Islamic Debate
Author explains the mission of the college and why logic is essential for debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is a prerequisite for Adab al-Bahth and identify the logical components (minor premise, major premise, middle term) mentioned in the passage.

Core Idea
Without logic, you cannot spot errors in a debate – that is why Adab al-Bahth requires prior training in logic.

The College of Da'wa and Usul al-Din was established to produce callers who can refute falsehood with wisdom. The author argues that to do this effectively, one must master the art of debate (Adab al-Bahth). However, because every argument in a debate is structured as a logical syllogism, understanding the rules of logic is essential. This lesson unpacks why logic is not an optional extra but a foundational tool for every da'wa worker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mission of the college
Read the first part of the passage: the college aims to produce callers capable of conveying the message with wisdom and confuting misguided opponents. What is the ultimate goal?
The goal is to establish truth and refute falsehood using customary methods.
2
Recognize the role of Adab al-Bahth
The passage then states that Adab al-Bahth is taught precisely because it enables a caller to point out errors in an opponent's argument and to construct a convincing proof for his own position. What specific abilities does this science give?
It teaches how to detect fallacies and correct one's own proof.
3
Understand why logic is needed
The author explains that every proof in a debate takes the form of a logical syllogism. Therefore, to understand objections like 'al-man' 'ala al-muqaddima' (objection to the premise) or criticisms about the middle term, one must know logic. What would happen if a debater had no knowledge of logic?
He would not understand the technical objections raised by the opponent.
4
Note the specific logical terms
The passage mentions three key terms: the minor premise (al-muqaddima al-sughra), the major premise (al-muqaddima al-kubra), and the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). Locate these terms in the Arabic and understand their function in a syllogism.
These are the building blocks of any categorical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباطل وإحقاق الحق على الطرق المتعارفة عند عامة الناس = حمل ذلك الجامعة على إنشاء كلية الدعوة وأصول الدين. ومهمتها تخريج دعاة قادرين على تبليغ الدعوة بالحكمة والموعظة الحسنة، وعلى إفحام وإلزام الدعاة المضللين، ببيان ما يصحح أدلتهم ، ويظهر بطلان حجج خصومهم . ومن أجل ذلك قررت في منهج هذه الكلية تدريس مادة آداب البحث والمناظرة ؛ لأنه هو العلم الذي يقدر به من تعلمه علی بیان مواضع الغلط في حُجة خصمه، وعلى تصحيح مذهبه بإقامة الدليل المقنع على صحته، أو صحة ملزومه، أو بطلان نقيضه، ونحو ذلك . ومن المعلوم أن المقدمات التي تتركب منها الأدلة التي يحتج بها كل واحد من المتناظرين إنما تُوجه الحجة بها منتظمة على صورة القياس المنطقي . ومن أجل ذلك كان فن آداب البحث والمناظرة» يتوقف فهمه كما ينبغي على فهم ما لابد منه من فن المنطق؛ لأن توجيه السائل المنع على المقدمة الصغرى أو الكبرى مثلاً ، أو القدح في الدليل بعدم تكرار الحد الوسط، أو باختلال شرط من شروط الإنتاج ونحو ذلك(١) ، لا يفهمه من لا إلمام له بفن المنطق .
And refuting falsehood and establishing truth according to the customary ways of the common people = that college undertook the establishment of the College of Da'wa and the Principles of Religion. Its mission is to produce callers capable of conveying the call with wisdom and good advice, and of confuting and compelling misguided callers, by explaining what corrects their proofs and showing the invalidity of their opponents' arguments. For that reason, the curriculum of this college decided to teach the subject of Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara (The Etiquette of Research and Debate); because it is the science by which the one who learns it is able to point out the places of error in his opponent's argument, and to correct his own position by establishing a convincing proof for its validity, or the validity of its implication, or the invalidity of its contradictory, and the like. And it is known that the premises from which the proofs used by each of the two disputants are composed are only directed as arguments in the form of a logical syllogism. For that reason, the art of Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara depends, for its proper understanding, on understanding what is necessary from the art of logic; because the questioner's directing of objection (al-man') to the minor or major premise, for example, or the criticism of the proof due to non-repetition of the middle term, or a violation of one of the conditions of productivity, and the like, is not understood by one who has no familiarity with the art of logic.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage explains the rationale for including logic in the curriculum of a college dedicated to Da'wa. The author first states the college's mission: to produce callers who can refute falsehood and establish truth using methods familiar to the common people. To do this effectively, callers must be able to identify errors in opponents' arguments and construct sound proofs. The passage then argues that the science of Adab al-Bahth (the etiquette of research and debate) is essential because it equips the learner to spot fallacies and defend positions convincingly. However, the author notes that the arguments in a debate are structured as logical syllogisms. Therefore, a proper understanding of debate requires prior knowledge of logic. Specific examples are given: a debater may object to a premise (minor or major), criticize an argument for not having a middle term, or point out a violation of the conditions for a valid syllogism. Without logic, these moves cannot be understood. Thus, the author concludes that logic is a prerequisite for mastering debate. This is a front_matter passage that sets the stage for the course, emphasizing the practical utility of logic for Islamic da'wa.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Why Purified Logic is Necessary for Disputation
Author defends studying purified logic for disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's defense of studying purified logic and its role in refuting heretical arguments against divine attributes.

Core Idea
Logic purified from philosophy is a necessary tool for Muslims to defend divine attributes against heretical syllogisms.

You may have heard criticisms of logic in Islam. This lesson shows how classical scholars distinguished between harmful Greek logic and the purified logical tools needed to refute false claims. The author of our text argues that once heretics began using syllogisms to deny Allah's attributes, Muslims had to learn logic to silence them with their own method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's stated purpose
Read the opening lines to find why the author wrote this memorandum. What does he say is necessary for students of disputation?
He says it is to enable students of the art to achieve their goal, starting with essential logical rules.
2
Extract the distinction between prohibited and permitted logic
Look at the poem by al-Mukhtar ibn Bun and the statements from al-Akhdari. How does the author distinguish between the logic of philosophers and the logic purified by Muslims?
The prohibited logic is that mixed with false philosophy; the permitted logic is that which has been purified by Muslim scholars and is necessary for knowledge.
3
Analyze the historical reasoning for learning logic
The author says that if logic had never been translated, Muslims would have no need for it. But it was translated and used to deny divine attributes. Why does this create a necessity for scholars to learn it?
4
Evaluate the argument for refutation by the same method
The author claims that the best way to silence a false opponent is to use the same kind of proof they accept. How does this justify learning logical syllogisms?
He says that refuting the heretics requires using the same logical form they employ, which makes their defeat inevitable.

والمناظرة، وكان لابد من وضع مذكرة تمكن طلاب الفن من مقصودهم، فوضعنا هذه المذكرة، وبدأناها بإيضاح القواعد التي لابد منها من فن المنطق لآداب البحث والمناظرة، واقتصرنا فيها على المهم الذي لابد منه للمناظرة، وجئنا بتلك الأصول المنطقية خالصةً من شوائب الشُّبه الفلسفية فيها النفع الذي لا يخالطه ضرر البتة؛ لأنها من الذي خلّصه علماء الإسلام من شوائب الفلسفة، كما قال العلامة شيخ مشايخنا وابن عمنا المختار بن بون ـ شارح الألفية والجامع معها ألفية أخرى من نظمه تكميلاً للفائدة ـ في نظمه في فن المنطق : فإن تقل حرمه النواوي وابن الصلاح والسيوطي الراوي قلت نرى الأقوال ذي المخالفة محلُّها ما صنف الفلاسفة أما الذي خلّصه من أسلما لابد أن يعلم عند العلما وأما قول الأخضري في سلمه : فابن الصلاح والنواوي حرما وقال قوم ينبغي أن يُعلما والقولةُ المشهورة الصحيحة جوازه لكامل القريحة ممارس السنّة والكتاب ليهتدي به إلى الصواب فمحله المنطق المشوب بكلام الفلاسفة الباطل. ومن المعلوم أن فن المنطق منذ تُرجم من اللغة اليونانية إلى اللغة العربية في أيام المأمون كانت جميع المؤلفات توجد فيها عبارات واصطلاحات منطقية لا يفهمها إلا من له إلمام به، ولا يفهم الردَّ على المنطقيين في ما جاؤوا به من الباطل إلا من له إلمام بفن المنطق . وقد يعين على رد الشبه التي جاء بها المتكلمون في أقيسة منطقية، فزعموا أن العقل يمنع بسببها كثيرًا من صفات الله الثابتة في الكتاب والسنة ؛ لأن أكبر سبب لإفحام المبطل أن تكون الحجة عليه من جنس يحتج به، وأن تكون مركبة من مقدمات على الهيأة التي يعترف الخصم المبطل بصحة إنتاجها . ما ولاشك أن المنطق لو لم يترجم إلى العربية ولم يتعلمه المسلمون لكان دينهم وعقيدته في غنى عنه، كما استغنى عنه سلفهم الصالح، ولكنه لما تُرجم وتُعلّم، وصارت أقيسته هي الطريق الوحيدة لنفي بعض صفات الله الثابتة في الوحيين كان ينبغي لعلماء المسلمين أن يتعلموه وينظروا فيه ليردّوا حجج المبطلين بجنس ما استدلوا به على نفيهم لبعض الصفات؛ لأن إفحامهم بنفس أدلتهم أدعى لانقطاعهم وإلزامهم الحق . واعلم أن نفس القياس المنطقي في حد ذاته صحيح النتائج، إن
And disputation, and it was necessary to produce a memorandum enabling students of the art to achieve their goal, so we produced this memorandum, and began it by clarifying the indispensable rules from the art of logic for the adab of research and disputation. We confined it to what is important and indispensable for disputation, and we presented those logical principles pure from the impurities of philosophical doubts, containing benefit untainted by any harm; because they are among what the scholars of Islam purified from the impurities of philosophy. As the scholar, the shaykh of our shaykhs and our cousin, al-Mukhtar ibn Bun — commentator on the Alfiyya and compiler of another Alfiyya from his own composition to complete the benefit — said in his poem on the art of logic: 'If you say al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah and al-Suyuti the narrator prohibited it, I say we consider the opposing statements: their place is what the philosophers authored. As for what the Muslims purified, it must be known among the scholars.' And as for the statement of al-Akhdari in his Sullam: 'Ibn al-Salah and al-Nawawi prohibited it, and a group said it should be taught.' And the well-known correct statement: its permissibility for one complete in intellect, practicing the Sunna and the Book, to be guided by it to correctness. So its place is logic mixed with the false speech of the philosophers. It is well known that the art of logic since it was translated from the Greek language into Arabic in the days of al-Ma'mun, all the works contained expressions and logical terminology that none understand except one who has familiarity with it, and none understands the refutation of the logicians regarding what they brought of falsehood except one who has familiarity with the art of logic. It may help in refuting the doubts that the theologians brought in logical syllogisms, claiming that reason prevents, because of them, many of God's attributes established in the Book and the Sunna; because the greatest reason for silencing the falsehood-holder is that the proof against him be of the same kind he uses as a proof, and that it be composed of premises in the form that the false opponent acknowledges as correctly producing. Undoubtedly, if logic had not been translated into Arabic and Muslims had not learned it, their religion and creed would have been independent of it, just as their righteous predecessors were independent of it. But once it was translated and taught, and its syllogisms became the only way to negate some of God's attributes established in the two revelations, it was incumbent upon Muslim scholars to learn it and examine it in order to refute the proofs of the falsehood-holders with the same kind they used to negate some attributes; because silencing them with their own proofs is more effective in cutting off their arguments and obliging them to accept the truth. Know that the logical syllogism itself, in its essence, is correct in its conclusions, if
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is the preface of the book, explaining the author's purpose and defending the study of logic. The author begins by stating the need for a memorandum on adab al-bahth (disputation), starting with essential logical rules purified from philosophical impurities. The logic presented is that which Muslim scholars have purified, not the philosophy-laden logic. The author quotes a poem by al-Mukhtar ibn Bun, who distinguishes between the logic of the philosophers (which was criticized by scholars like al-Nawawi and Ibn al-Salah) and the logic purified by Muslims, which is necessary knowledge. Another quote from al-Akhdari's Sullam notes the disagreement on permissibility, and the author clarifies that the permissible logic is that which is practiced by one complete in intellect and adherence to the Sunna and Qur'an, not the corrupted logic. The author then gives a historical note: since logic was translated, its terminology became widespread, and refuting false logicians requires understanding it. Moreover, the theologians (mutakallimun) used logical syllogisms to deny attributes of Allah, so Muslims must learn logic to refute them with their own type of argument. The greatest way to silence a false opponent is to use the same kind of proof they employ, in the form they acknowledge as valid. The author concedes that if logic had not entered the Islamic world, Muslims would have no need for it. But now that it is used to deny divine attributes, scholars must learn it to defend the truth. The final sentence affirms that logical syllogism in itself yields correct conclusions, but the text cuts off, suggesting further discussion. Overall, this is a defense of studying logic for dialectical purposes, emphasizing purification and utility in defending creed.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Necessity of Learning Logic to Defend the Creed
Author argues for learning logic to refute heretical attacks on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument for why Muslims must learn logic to refute heretical denials of divine attributes.

Core Idea
The tool of logic is neutral; misuse begets error, but correct use affirms revelation.

This passage addresses a critical juncture in Islamic intellectual history: the introduction of Greek logic. The author argues that while logic was not originally needed, its adoption by heretics to attack divine attributes made it indispensable for orthodox scholars. He insists that the syllogism itself is sound when properly formed, and errors arise from mistaken assumptions of necessary concomitance. The key is to learn the tool to refute falsehood with its own methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Heretics (mutakallimun) use logical syllogisms to claim that reason negates certain divine attributes established in Quran and Sunnah. The author states this as the motivation for learning logic.
The heretics' arguments are presented as 'shubuhat' (doubts) framed in logical form.
2
Recognize the Tool's Neutrality
The author affirms that the logical syllogism in itself is sound: if premises are correct in matter and form, the conclusion is certain. The flaw lies in the user's error.
He gives the example of mistakenly assuming a necessary concomitance (mulazamah) between two things when none exists.
3
Understand the Author's Response Strategy
The most effective way to silence the falsifier is to rebut him using the same type of argument he employs. Scholars must master logic to refute heretics on their own terms.
The author emphasizes using 'the same kind' of evidence ('bijinsi ma istadallu bihi').
4
Note the Historical Contingency
The author clarifies that if logic had never entered Islamic civilization, Muslims would have been fine without it, as the righteous predecessors were. But since it did, and became the basis for denying attributes, scholars must learn it.
This shows the argument is not absolute but contextual.
5
Grasp the Error Leading to Contradiction with Revelation
When a syllogism's conclusion contradicts revelation, it is due to the arguer's error (e.g., false mulazamah). If used correctly, the syllogism would match revelation, as sound reason does not contradict authentic transmission.
The author asserts harmony of reason and revelation when logic is soundly applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقولةُ المشهورة الصحيحة جوازه لكامل القريحة ممارس السنّة والكتاب ليهتدي به إلى الصواب فمحله المنطق المشوب بكلام الفلاسفة الباطل. ومن المعلوم أن فن المنطق منذ تُرجم من اللغة اليونانية إلى اللغة العربية في أيام المأمون كانت جميع المؤلفات توجد فيها عبارات واصطلاحات منطقية لا يفهمها إلا من له إلمام به، ولا يفهم الردَّ على المنطقيين في ما جاؤوا به من الباطل إلا من له إلمام بفن المنطق . وقد يعين على رد الشبه التي جاء بها المتكلمون في أقيسة منطقية، فزعموا أن العقل يمنع بسببها كثيرًا من صفات الله الثابتة في الكتاب والسنة ؛ لأن أكبر سبب لإفحام المبطل أن تكون الحجة عليه من جنس يحتج به، وأن تكون مركبة من مقدمات على الهيأة التي يعترف الخصم المبطل بصحة إنتاجها . ما ولاشك أن المنطق لو لم يترجم إلى العربية ولم يتعلمه المسلمون لكان دينهم وعقيدتهم في غنى عنه، كما استغنى عنه سلفهم الصالح، ولكنه لما تُرجم وتُعلّم، وصارت أقيسته هي الطريق الوحيدة لنفي بعض صفات الله الثابتة في الوحيين كان ينبغي لعلماء المسلمين أن يتعلموه وينظروا فيه ليردّوا حجج المبطلين بجنس ما استدلوا به على نفيهم لبعض الصفات؛ لأن إفحامهم بنفس أدلتهم أدعى لانقطاعهم وإلزامهم الحق . واعلم أن نفس القياس المنطقي في حد ذاته صحيح النتائج، إن ركبت مقدماته على الوجه الصحيح صورة ومادة مع شروط إنتاجه فهو قطعي الصحة، وإنما يعتريه الخلل من جهة الناظر فيه، فيغلط، فيظن هذا الأمر لازما لهذا مثلاً ، فيستدل بنفي ذلك اللازم في زعمه على نفي ذلك الملزوم، مع أنه لا ملازمة بينهما في نفس الأمر البتة. ومن أجل غلطه في ذلك تخرج النتيجة مخالفة للوحي الصحيح؛ لغلط المستدل ولو كان استعماله للقياس المنطقي على الوجه الصحيح لكانت نتيجته مطابقة للوحي بلا شك؛ لأن العقل الصحيح لا يخالف النقل الصريح
The well-known correct statement: its permissibility is for the one with complete natural intelligence, practicing the Sunnah and the Book, to be guided by it to what is correct. Its place is logic mixed with the false speech of the philosophers. It is well-known that the art of logic, since it was translated from the Greek language into Arabic in the days of al-Ma'mun, all authored works contained logical expressions and terminologies that no one understands except one who has familiarity with it, and one does not understand the refutation of the logicians in what they brought of falsehood except one who has familiarity with the art of logic. And it may assist in refuting the doubts that the mutakallimun brought in logical syllogisms, claiming that reason prevents, because of them, many of God's attributes established in the Book and the Sunnah; because the greatest cause for silencing the falsifier is that the argument against him be of the kind he uses to argue, and that it be composed of premises in the form that the falsifying opponent acknowledges the validity of their production. No doubt that if logic had not been translated into Arabic and Muslims had not learned it, their religion and creed would have been independent of it, just as their righteous predecessors were independent of it. But since it was translated and learned, and its syllogisms became the sole way to negate some of God's attributes established in the two revelations, it was incumbent upon the Muslim scholars to learn it and examine it to refute the arguments of the falsifiers with the same kind of evidence they used to negate some attributes; because silencing them with their own evidence is more conducive to their being cut off and forced to accept the truth. And know that the logical syllogism itself, in its essence, produces correct conclusions. If its premises are constructed correctly in form and matter with the conditions of its production, it is definitively sound. Flaw only afflicts it from the perspective of the one examining it, so he errs and thinks that one thing is a necessary concomitant of another, for example, then uses the negation of that supposed necessary concomitant to negate the original, even though there is no concomitance between them at all in reality. Because of his error in that, the result comes out contradicting the sound revelation, due to the error of the arguer. Had he used the logical syllogism correctly, its result would undoubtedly match the revelation, because sound reason does not contradict explicit transmission.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument establishing the necessity of learning the science of logic for Muslim scholars, particularly to defend creedal truths against sophistic attacks. The author begins by stating a well-known saying: that logic is only permissible for one who already has sound natural intelligence and practices the Sunnah and Quran, because logic mixed with false philosophy can mislead. He then notes that since the translation of Greek logic into Arabic in the time of Caliph al-Ma'mun, all scholarly works contain logical terminology, and only those familiar with logic can understand the refutations against the falsehood brought by logicians. Moreover, the doubts raised by the mutakallimun (theologians) against divine attributes are couched in logical syllogisms, so the only way to effectively refute them is to use the same kind of argumentation. The author emphasizes that if logic had never entered the Islamic world, Muslims would have been fine without it, as were their righteous predecessors. But since it did enter and became the basis for denying some attributes of Allah, scholars must learn it to refute false arguments using the opponents' own tools. Next, the author clarifies that logical syllogism itself is sound—if its premises are correctly formed (in matter and form), its conclusion is certain. The problem arises from the user's error, such as mistakenly assuming a necessary concomitance (luzum) between two things when none exists. This error leads to conclusions that contradict revelation. But if the syllogism is used correctly, its conclusion will always agree with the sound revelation, because sound reason and authentic revelation cannot conflict. This argument is crucial for defending the harmony of reason and revelation in Islamic thought.

Key Terms
المنطق
القياس المنطقي
الملازمة
المقدمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the Quran commands the study of Greek philosophy.

Key Takeaway
Muslim scholars must learn the logical methods of their opponents to refute them effectively, while recognizing that the tool itself is neutral and its misuse is the source of error.

The Author's Three-Part Plan for Adab al-Bahth
Author outlines the book's plan after logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three main sections of the book after the preliminary logic, as outlined by the author.

Core Idea
The book applies logical disputation rules to fiqh and kalam to train the student in refuting falsehood.

In this passage, the author announces the structure of the remainder of the book. Having completed the necessary groundwork in logic, he now prepares to guide the student through practical applications. Understanding this plan helps you see how logical tools are not merely theoretical but are meant to be wielded in real Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three sections
Read the passage and identify the three topics the author will cover after the logic section. The author uses phrases like 'ثم نطبق ذلك' (then we apply that) and 'ونختتم الكلام بالمقارنة' (and we conclude with a comparison).
The three sections are: (1) adab al-bahth wal-munazara, (2) application to usul al-fiqh and kalam, (3) comparison of Salaf and Khalaf.
2
Extract the purpose of each section
For each section, note what the author says it will achieve. For adab al-bahth: it helps correct one's doctrine and refute opponents. For the application: it clarifies truth and refutes falsehood in specific issues. For the comparison: it provides training in rebutting doubts.
The purpose evolves from learning rules to applying them to concrete cases to practical training.
3
Recognize the pedagogical progression
Observe the sequence: first, the student learns the rules of disputation; then sees them applied in usul al-fiqh and kalam; finally, engages in a comparative analysis of Salaf and Khalaf. This is designed to build competence step by step.
The author emphasizes that practical application ('تطبيق ذلك عملياً') benefits the student greatly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد الانتهاء مما لابد منه من فن المنطق نذكر جملا كافية من آداب البحث والمناظرة تُعين من تعلمها على تصحيح مذهبه وإبطال مذهب خصمه، مع الآداب اللازمة لذلك ، ثم نطبق ذلك في مسائل من القوادح في أصول الفقه، ومسائل من مسائل الكلام التي نفى فيها المعطلون بعض الصفات، ونوضح كيفية تصحيح الحق في ذلك وإبطال الباطل ؛ لأن تطبيق ذلك عملياً يفيد الطالب إفادة أكبر . ونختتم الكلام بالمقارنة بين ما يسميه المتكلمون مذهب السلف ومذهب الخلف، مع إحقاق الحق وإبطال الباطل على الطرق المعهودة في المناظرة؛ ليفيدَ ذلك الطالب تمرينا على رد الشبه وإبطال الباطل بطريق المناظرة . وهذا أوان الشروع في ذكر ما لابد منه من المسائل المنطقية .
المؤلف
And after concluding what is necessary from the art of logic, we mention sufficient statements from the etiquettes of research and disputation (adab al-bahth wal-munazara) that help the one who learns them to correct his own doctrine and refute the doctrine of his opponent, along with the necessary etiquettes for that. Then we apply that to issues regarding the invalidating objections (qawadih) in usul al-fiqh, and issues from kalam in which the negators (mu'attila) negated some attributes, and we clarify how to establish the truth therein and refute falsehood, because applying that practically benefits the student in a greater way. And we conclude the discussion with a comparison between what the mutakallimun call the doctrine of the Salaf and the doctrine of the Khalaf, with the establishment of truth and refutation of falsehood according to the customary methods of disputation, so that this benefits the student as an exercise in rebutting doubts and refuting falsehood through the method of disputation. This is the time to commence mentioning the necessary logical issues. The author.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the remainder of the book, outlining the author's pedagogical plan. Having completed the essential elements of logic, the author announces his intention to present a sufficient collection of rules from 'adab al-bahth wal-munazara' (the etiquette of research and disputation). These rules are designed to equip the student with the tools to correct his own doctrinal position and to refute the position of his opponent, while adhering to the proper manners of debate.

The author then explains that he will apply these rules to two specific areas: first, to issues concerning 'al-qawadih' (invalidating objections) in usul al-fiqh, and second, to issues in kalam where the mu'attila (those who negate divine attributes) have negated some of God's attributes. The aim is to demonstrate how to establish truth and refute falsehood. The author emphasizes the practical value of this application, noting that it yields greater benefit for the student than mere theoretical knowledge.

Finally, the author states that he will conclude with a comparison between what the mutakallimun (theologians) call the doctrine of the Salaf (the early generations) and the doctrine of the Khalaf (the later generations). This comparison is to be conducted by establishing truth and refuting falsehood through the customary methods of disputation, providing the student with practical training in rebutting doubts and refuting falsehood. The author then signals the transition to the main content by declaring that it is now time to begin mentioning the necessary logical issues.

The Author's Roadmap: Logic, Debate, and Applied Theology
Author describes the purpose of the book: from logic to applied debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the three main stages of the author's project and identify the two fields where the rules of debate are applied.

Core Idea
Debate rules are not just theory; they must be tested on usul al-fiqh and kalam.

The author lays out his plan for the book. After laying the logical foundation, he will present the rules of debate (ādāb al-baḥth wa al-munāẓara), then apply them to actual problems in usul al-fiqh and kalam. Finally, he will compare the salaf and khalaf methodologies to demonstrate how truth is established through dialectic. This lesson helps you see the overall structure and purpose.

Guided Path

The Three Essentials of Munazara: Division, Conversion, Contradiction
Author limits the discussion to three essential topics for debate: quantity/quality division, conversion, and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential topics for munazara and explain why other logical topics are omitted in this context.

Core Idea
Effective disputation requires only three logical tools: propositional division, conversion, and contradiction.

The author of this text on the art of disputation (munazara) has been systematically classifying propositions. Now he makes a strategic decision: rather than covering every logical nuance, he will focus only on what is strictly necessary for structured debate. This lesson unpacks that decision, showing you the three core topics that form the foundation of dialectical argumentation in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
1. Recap the proposition classifications
Recall that the author divided propositions into categorical and hypothetical, then further subdivided hypotheticals into conditionals and disjunctives. He explains each division with examples. Notice that this detailed classification is background knowledge, not the focus of munazara.
2
2. Notice what is omitted
The author explicitly lists topics he will NOT discuss: the copula (because Arabic does not need it), modal propositions, irregular quantifiers, the real vs. extrinsic distinction, and positive vs. negative propositions. Understand that these are important in general logic but not essential for the practical purposes of debate.
This is a deliberate pedagogical narrowing, not a dismissal of these topics.
3
3. Identify the three essentials
The author declares that only three things are necessary for munazara: (1) division of propositions by quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative), (2) conversion, and (3) contradiction. Commit these to memory as the core tools for disputation.
4
4. Understand the rationale
Reflect on why these three suffice. Division by quantity/quality allows you to correctly categorize the opponent's claims. Conversion lets you derive equivalent propositions from the opponent's statements. Contradiction is the primary method of refutation. Together, they cover the key moves in a debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت مما ذكرنا أن القضية تنقسم إلى حملية وشرطية بالتقسيم الأول، وأنّ الشرطية تنقسم إلى متصلة ومنفصلة، وأن المتصلة تنقسم إلى لزومية واتفاقية وأن المنفصلة تنقسم إلى حقيقية مانعة جمع وخلو معا، ومانعة جمع مجوزة خلو، ومانعة خلو مجوزة جمع، وعرفت أنا بينا كل قسم بأمثلته، فاعلم أنا أردنا هنا أن نقتصر على ما لابد منه في علم المناظرة فلم نذكر الرابطة؛ لعدم الاحتياج لها في اللغة العربية للاكتفاء عنها بالاشتقاق والإضافة مثلاً . ولم نتعرض للجهة ولا للقضايا الموجهة، ولا لمنحرفات السور، ولا للحقيقيات والخارجيات، ولا للمحصلات والمعدولات، إلى غير ذلك من أحكام القضايا، ولكنا أردنا هنا أن نبين ما لابد للمناظرة منه، وهو ثلاثة أشياء، وبها ينتهي بحثنا في مبادىء التصديقات : الأول : تقسيم القضايا باعتبار الكم والكيف خاصة . الثاني : العكس . الثالث : التناقض.
And if you have understood from what we mentioned that the proposition is divided into categorical and hypothetical according to the first division, and that the hypothetical is divided into conditional and disjunctive, and that the conditional is divided into implicative and accidental, and that the disjunctive is divided into the true [disjunction] which prevents both combination and emptiness together, and the one which prevents combination but permits emptiness, and the one which prevents emptiness but permits combination; and you have known that we have explained each division with its examples, then know that we intend here to restrict ourselves to what is necessary in the science of disputation. Thus we did not mention the copula because it is not needed in the Arabic language, since [Arabic] suffices with derivation and annexation for it. We did not discuss modality, nor modal propositions, nor irregular quantifiers, nor the real and the extrinsic, nor the positive and the negative, and other such rules of propositions. Rather, we intend here to clarify what is necessary for disputation, and that is three things, and with them ends our discussion on the principles of assents: First: the division of propositions with respect to quantity and quality alone. Second: conversion. Third: contradiction.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage serves as a methodological transition. The author recaps the classification of propositions: categorical and hypothetical; hypothetical divides into conditional (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila); conditional into implicative (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya); disjunctive into three types: true disjunction (haqiqiyya) which is both exclusive and exhaustive, exclusive-only (mani'at al-jam' al-mujawwizat al-khuluw), and exhaustive-only (mani'at al-khuluw al-mujawwizat li al-jam'). Then he explains that for the purpose of munazara, he will focus only on what is strictly necessary. Thus he omits: the copula (since Arabic doesn't need it due to verbal conjugation and annexation), modality (jiha) and modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha), irregular quantifiers (munharifat al-sawr), the distinction between real and extrinsic propositions (haqiqiyyat and kharijiyyat), positive and negative propositions (muhassalat and ma'dulat), and other details. Instead, he will cover three essential topics: first, the division of propositions by quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative); second, conversion (al-aks); third, contradiction (al-tanaqud). These three form the principles of assents (mabadi' al-tasdiqat) necessary for dialectical argumentation.

المناظرة
العكس
التناقض

Scope of Munazara: The Three Necessary Topics in Tasdiqat
Author states the plan to limit discussion to three foundational topics in propositions for dialectics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author focuses on only three operations in propositional logic for munazara and identify which topics he deliberately omits.

Core Idea
The author narrows propositional logic to three tools essential for dialectical debate.

In this passage, the author sets the stage for the science of munazara by delineating exactly which logical concepts he will cover. He reviews the familiar division of propositions into categorical and conditional, but then explicitly limits the discussion to three topics: division by quantity and quality, conversion, and contradiction. Understanding this roadmap will help you see how the subsequent lessons fit into the broader framework of dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's overall purpose
The author states he intends to restrict himself to what is necessary for the science of dialectics (al-munazara). What is the overarching goal of this restriction?
The goal is to provide only the essential logical tools needed for debate, avoiding extraneous details.
2
List the omitted topics
The author explicitly mentions what he is not covering: the copula (al-rabita), modality, modal propositions, deviant quantifiers, essential/existential propositions, and affirmative/negated propositions. Why does he omit these?
He omits them because they are not necessary for the practical science of munazara; the Arabic language itself handles the copula through derivation and annexation.
3
Extract the three necessary topics
The author enumerates three things in the foundations of assents (mabadi' al-tasdiqat). What are they? Write them down as they appear.
The three are: (1) division of propositions by quantity and quality, (2) conversion (al-'aks), and (3) contradiction (al-tanaqud).
4
Understand the sequence
After these three topics, the author will discuss the purposes of assents (maqasid al-tasdiqat) and then proceed to the main subject of Adab al-Bahth. How does this structure serve the student of dialectics?
This structure builds from foundational logical operations to higher-level applications, ensuring the student masters essential tools before engaging in full debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسود قطعا كما هو ضرورة . وإذا علمت مما ذكرنا أن القضية تنقسم إلى حملية وشرطية بالتقسيم الأول، وأن الشرطية تنقسم إلى متصلة ومنفصلة، وأن المتصلة تنقسم إلى لزومية واتفاقية وأن المنفصلة تنقسم إلى حقيقية مانعة جمع وخلو معا، ومانعة جمع مجوزة خلو، ومانعة خلو مجوزة جمع، وعرفت أنا بينا كل قسم بأمثلته، فاعلم أنا أردنا هنا أن نقتصر على ما لابد منه في علم المناظرة فلم نذكر الرابطة؛ لعدم الاحتياج لها في اللغة العربية للاكتفاء عنها بالاشتقاق والإضافة مثلا . ولم نتعرض للجهة ولا للقضايا الموجهة، ولا لمنحرفات السور، ولا للحقيقيات والخارجيات، ولا للمحصلات والمعدولات، إلى غير ذلك من أحكام القضايا، ولكنا أردنا هنا أن نبين مالابد للمناظرة منه، وهو ثلاثة أشياء، وبها ينتهي بحثنا في مبادىء التصديقات : الأول : تقسيم القضايا باعتبار الكم والكيف خاصة . الثاني : العكس . الثالث : التناقض . ثم نذكر مقاصد التصديقات، ثم نشرع في المقصود من آداب البحث والمناظرة .
Absolutely black as is necessary. And if you know from what we have mentioned that the proposition is divided into categorical and conditional by the first division, and that the conditional is divided into conjunctive and disjunctive, and that the conjunctive is divided into implicative and accidental, and that the disjunctive is divided into the true one preventing both conjunction and disjunction together, preventing conjunction while permitting disjunction, and preventing disjunction while permitting conjunction, and you know that we explained each division with its examples, then know that we intended here to restrict ourselves to what is necessary in the science of dialectics, so we did not mention the copula because there is no need for it in the Arabic language, being satisfied with derivation and annexation instead. And we did not deal with modality, nor with modal propositions, nor with deviant quantifiers, nor with essential and existential propositions, nor with affirmative and negated propositions, and other such rulings of propositions. But we intended here to clarify what is necessary for dialectics, which are three things, and with them our discussion in the foundations of assents ends: First: division of propositions with respect to quantity and quality specifically. Second: conversion. Third: contradiction. Then we will mention the purposes of assents, then we will begin the intended subject of the etiquette of investigation and dialectics.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement where the author explains the scope of his work on munazara (dialectics). He reminds the reader of the previous division of propositions into categorical and conditional, and the subdivisions of conditionals (conjunctive/disjunctive, etc.). Then he states that he will only cover what is absolutely necessary for the science of debate, omitting many details of propositional logic such as the copula (because Arabic does not need it due to derivation and annexation), modality, modal propositions, deviant quantifiers, essential vs. existential propositions, and affirmative vs. negated propositions. Instead, he will focus on three foundational topics in the 'mabadi' al-tasdiqat' (foundations of assents): (1) division of propositions by quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative), (2) conversion (aks), and (3) contradiction (tanaqud). After this, he will discuss the 'maqasid al-tasdiqat' (purposes of assents) and then proceed to the main subject of Adab al-Bahth wal-Munazara (the etiquette of investigation and dialectics). The passage serves as a roadmap for the reader, clarifying what will and will not be covered, and emphasizing the practical focus on dialectical reasoning.

Definitions of Bahth, Munazara, and Nazar
Author linguistically and terminologically defines bahth, munazara, and nazar, citing Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the linguistic and terminological definitions of bahth, munazara, and nazar as given by the author, and identify how they relate to the Islamic practice of debate.

Core Idea
Bahth, munāẓarah, and naẓar form the conceptual foundation of Islamic dialectical inquiry.

This opening passage defines the three key terms of the discipline: bahth (investigation), munāẓarah (debate), and naẓar (reflection). The author carefully distinguishes linguistic and technical meanings, grounding the legitimacy of debate in the Qur'an. Understanding these definitions is essential before diving into the rules of proper argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition of bahth
Look for the phrase 'في اللغة الفحص والتفتيش' and the Quranic example of the raven scratching the earth. This shows the root meaning of scrutiny.
The Quranic verse illustrates bahth as a physical scratching, extended metaphorically to intellectual investigation.
2
Identify the terminological definition of munāẓarah
Find 'في الاصطلاح : المحاورة في الكلام بين شخصين مختلفين...' and note the key elements: dialogue, two differing persons, each aiming to validate his own statement and invalidate the other's, both desiring truth.
The linguistic definition is simpler (confrontation), while the terminological adds the truth-seeking purpose.
3
Understand the role of naẓar
See how naẓar (reflection) underpins both bahth and munāẓarah as the mental process leading to knowledge (ʿilm) or prevailing opinion (ghalabat al-ẓann).
Naẓar is the cognitive engine; without it, debate would be mere argumentation without intellectual outcome.
4
Recognize the Quranic basis
Note that the author cites verse 16:125 'And argue with them in a way that is best' to legitimize munāzara as a sanctioned activity.
This grounds the practice in revelation, distinguishing it from prohibited contentious argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
آداب البحث والمناظرة
اعلم أولاً أن البحث في اللغة الفحص والتفتيش. ومنه قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ فَبَعَتَ اللهُ غُرَابًا يَبْحَثُ فِي الْأَرْضِ .. الآية [المائدة/ ٣١]، والمتناظران كل منهما يفحص ويفتش عما يصحح به حجته ويبطل به حجة خصمه
والمناظرة : مفاعلة على بابها من اقتضاء الطرفين، وهي من النظر أو النظير، وكلاهما معروف لغة .
والنظر في الاصطلاح : هو الفكر المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن. وقد قدمنا تعريفه وتعريف الفكر في الاصطلاح
فالمناظرة في اللغة : المقابلة بين اثنين كل منهما ينظر إلى الآخر، أو كل منهما ينظر بمعنى يفكر ، والفكر هو المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن .
وهي في الاصطلاح : المحاورة في الكلام بين شخصين مختلفين يقصد كل واحد منهما تصحيح قوله وإبطال قول الآخر، مع رغبة كل منهما في ظهور الحق، فكأنها بالمعنى الاصطلاحي مشاركتهما في النظر الذي هو الفكر المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن ليظهر الصواب . والأصل في مشروعيتها قوله تعالى : ( وَحَدِ لَهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ)
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The etiquette of investigation and debate. Know first that 'al-bahth' in language means scrutiny and examination. From it is the saying of the Exalted: 'Then Allah sent a raven scratching the earth...' [al-Ma'idah 5:31]. The two disputants each scrutinize and examine what validates their argument and invalidates the argument of their opponent. 'Al-munazara' is a mutual interaction (mufa'ala) in its original sense of both parties demanding, and it is derived from 'al-nazar' or 'al-nazir', both known in language. 'Al-nazar' in terminology is reflection leading to knowledge or prevailing opinion. We have previously presented its definition and the definition of reflection in terminology. Thus 'al-munazara' in language is the confrontation between two, each of whom looks at the other, or each of whom reflects meaning thinks, and reflection is what leads to knowledge or prevailing opinion. And it (al-munazara) in terminology is verbal dialogue between two differing persons, each intending to validate his statement and invalidate the other's statement, along with each one's desire for the manifestation of truth. So it is, in the terminological sense, as if they both participate in reflection (al-nazar) which is the reflection leading to knowledge or prevailing opinion, so that correctness may appear. The basis for its legitimacy is the saying of the Exalted: 'And argue with them in a way that is best' [al-Nahl 16:125].
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage opens the book 'Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara' by defining the foundational terms. It is a front-matter passage serving as a terminological and legitimizing introduction. The author first defines 'al-bahth' (investigation) linguistically as 'al-fahṣ wa al-taftīsh' (scrutiny and examination), citing the Quranic verse where the raven 'yabḥathu fī al-arḍ' (scratches the earth) from Surah al-Ma'idah. He then links this to the activity of the two disputants (al-mutanāẓirān), each scrutinizing to support his own proof and refute the opponent's. Next, he defines 'al-munāẓarah' (debate) as a mutual action (mufā‘alah) implying two parties, deriving from either 'al-naẓar' (looking/reflecting) or 'al-naẓīr' (counterpart), both common in language. He then gives the terminological definition of 'al-naẓar' (reflection) as thought (al-fikr) leading to certain knowledge ('ilm) or prevailing opinion (ghalabat al-ẓann), noting that this definition was previously provided. He repeats the linguistic definition of munāẓarah as a confrontation of two persons each looking at the other or each thinking, and then provides the terminological definition: a verbal dialogue (muḥāwarah) between two differing individuals, each intending to correct his own statement and invalidate the other's, with both desiring the emergence of truth. It is thus a shared participation in reflection that leads to knowledge or prevailing opinion, so that correctness (ṣawāb) may appear. The passage concludes by citing the Quranic basis for the legitimacy of debate: 'And argue with them in a way that is best' (Surah al-Nahl, 16:125). The author uses this verse to ground the practice in revelation, emphasizing that munāzara is not mere argumentation but a truth-seeking method sanctioned by Allah.

Key Terms
البحث (al-bahth): Linguistically: scrutiny and examination (al-fahṣ wa al-taftīsh). In the context of this work, it refers to the activity of disputants investigating arguments. [Note: In later texts, al-bahth may become a technical term for logical investigation, but here it is simply the linguistic root.]
المناظرة (al-munāẓarah): In language: confrontation between two persons each looking at or thinking about the other. In terminology: verbal dialogue between two differing individuals, each aiming to validate his own statement and invalidate the opponent's, while both seek truth. [Note: In later dialectical works (e.g., Ādāb al-Baḥth of al-Samarqandī), munāẓarah becomes a heavily codified art with specific rules; here the definition is more general.]
النظر (al-naẓar): Terminologically: reflection (al-fikr) leading to knowledge or prevailing opinion. It is the mental act that underpins both investigation and debate. [Note: In logic (al-manṭiq), al-naẓar often denotes speculative reasoning or deduction; here it is broader, encompassing any thought process leading to cognitive results.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Reflection leading to knowledge
B) Scrutiny and examination
C) Verbal dialogue between two persons
D) Mutual confrontation

Key Takeaway
The author establishes that investigation and debate are rooted in language and legitimated by the Quran, and that reflection (naẓar) is the underlying cognitive process.

Linguistic and Technical Definitions of Bahth and Munazarah
Author defines 'bahth' and 'munazarah' linguistically and technically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'bahth' and 'munazarah' linguistically and technically, and explain how 'nazar' connects to the technical meaning of munazarah.

Core Idea
Bahth and munazarah are not just arguing; they are disciplined investigations seeking truth.

The terms 'bahth' (بحث) and 'munazarah' (مناظرة) are foundational to the Islamic discipline of debate. This lesson examines their linguistic roots, including a Qur'anic usage of 'bahth', and moves to their technical meanings. Understanding these definitions is essential before studying the rules of adab al-bahth.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the linguistic meaning of 'bahth'
The author defines bahth linguistically as 'examination and investigation' (الفحص والتفتيش). He cites Qur'an 5:31 where a raven scratches the earth – the same root ب-ح-ث. Connect this to the idea of uncovering something hidden.
2
Apply this to the disputants
The two disputants (المتناظران) each use bahth to validate their own argument and invalidate the opponent's. So bahth in debate retains the sense of investigation but is now purposeful: to strengthen one's own position and weaken the other's.
3
Examine the linguistic definition of 'munazarah'
Munazarah is on the pattern mufa'alah, indicating reciprocity. It is derived from 'nazar' (looking/thinking) or 'nadhir' (similar). Linguistically it means two people confronting each other, each looking at the other or thinking.
4
Connect 'nazar' to the technical definition
The author provides the technical definition of 'nazar': thought that leads to knowledge or overwhelming probability (الفكر المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن). This is the key to the technical definition of munazarah: it is dialogue between two persons with mutual desire to find truth, sharing in nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أولاً أن البحث في اللغة الفحص والتفتيش. ومنه قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ فَبَعَثَ اللهُ غُرَابًا يَبْحَثُ فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ [المائدة/ ٣١]، والمتناظران كل منهما يفحص ويفتش عما يصحح به حجته ويبطل به حجة خصمه. والمناظرة : مفاعلة على بابها من اقتضاء الطرفين، وهي من النظر أو النظير، وكلاهما معروف لغة . والنظر في الاصطلاح : هو الفكر المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن. وقد قدمنا تعريفه وتعريف الفكر في الاصطلاح. فالمناظرة في اللغة : المقابلة بين اثنين كل منهما ينظر إلى الآخر، أو كل منهما ينظر بمعنى يفكر ، والفكر هو المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن . وهي في الاصطلاح : المحاورة في الكلام بين شخصين مختلفين يقصد كل واحد منهما تصحيح قوله وإبطال قول الآخر، مع رغبة كل منهما في ظهور الحق، فكأنها بالمعنى الاصطلاحي مشاركتهما في النظر الذي هو الفكر المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن ليظهر الصواب .
Know first that 'bahth' in language means examination and investigation. From it is the saying of Allah, the Exalted: 'Then Allah sent a raven scratching in the earth' [Al-Ma'idah 5:31]. The two disputants each examine and investigate what can validate his argument and invalidate his opponent's argument. 'Munazarah' is on the pattern of mufa'alah, from the requirement of two parties, and it is from 'nazar' or 'nadhir', both known in language. 'Nazar' in technical terminology is the thought that leads to knowledge or overwhelming probability. We have already presented its definition and the definition of 'fikr' in terminology. So 'munazarah' in language is the confrontation between two, each looking at the other, or each reflecting, meaning thinking; and 'fikr' is what leads to knowledge or overwhelming probability. In technical terminology it is dialogue in speech between two different persons, each intending to validate his own statement and invalidate the other's statement, with each desiring the appearance of truth; so it is, in the technical sense, their sharing in 'nazar', which is the thought that leads to knowledge or overwhelming probability, so that correctness may appear.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and technical definition of two key terms: 'bahth' (بحث) and 'munazarah' (مناظرة). The author begins by stating the linguistic meaning of 'bahth': it is examination and investigation (الفحص والتفتيش). He supports this with the Qur'anic verse about the raven scratching the earth (Al-Ma'idah 5:31). He then notes that the two disputants (المتناظران) each engage in such examination to support their own argument and refute their opponent's. Next, the author defines 'munazarah' linguistically: it is on the pattern of mufa'alah, indicating reciprocity between two parties, derived from either 'nazar' (looking/thinking) or 'nadhir' (similar). The technical definition of 'nazar' is given as 'the thought that leads to knowledge or overwhelming probability' (الفكر المؤدي إلى علم أو غلبة ظن), which he references from earlier in the book. Applying this, the linguistic definition of 'munazarah' is 'the confrontation between two, each looking at the other or each thinking', and the technical definition is 'dialogue in speech between two different persons, each intending to validate his own statement and invalidate the other's, with mutual desire for the appearance of truth'. Thus it is essentially a shared intellectual effort towards correctness.

Key Terms
بحث (bahth): Linguistically: examination and investigation. Technically: the method of inquiry used in disputation to validate one's argument and invalidate the opponent's. [Note: In later adab al-bahth works, 'bahth' often refers specifically to the structured process of debate.]
مناظرة (munazarah): Linguistically: reciprocal confrontation or thinking between two. Technically: dialogue aimed at validating one's own position and invalidating the opponent's, with mutual pursuit of truth. [Note: In other contexts, 'munazarah' may refer to any scholarly debate, but here it is explicitly tied to 'nazar' as thought.]
نظر (nazar): Technical: thought that leads to knowledge or overwhelming probability (fikr mu'addin ila 'ilm aw ghalabat zann). [Note: In logic, 'nazar' often overlaps with 'fikr'; here it is defined distinctly as the product of thought.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The terms 'bahth' and 'munazarah' are defined linguistically as investigation and reciprocal confrontation, and technically as purposeful dialogue aimed at truth through shared intellectual inquiry (nazar).

Defining the Subject of Adab al-Bahth
Author defines the subject of this art as universal investigations containing partial investigations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject of Adab al-Bahth and explain the two evaluative conditions (directed and accepted) that govern arguments within this discipline.

Core Idea
Adab al-Bahth studies not the content of arguments, but the formal conditions that make them acceptable in a debate.

In this brief but foundational passage, the author tells us exactly what this discipline is about. It is not about any particular topic; rather, it is about the universal rules that govern how we conduct inquiry and debate. The key is understanding that the subject is 'universal investigations' that contain 'partial investigations' and that these are evaluated by whether they are 'directed and accepted' or not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject (موضوع) of the art
Find the phrase 'وموضوع هذا الفن' and then what follows: 'الأبحاث الكلية'. So the subject is universal investigations.
2
Understand the relationship between universal and partial investigations
The clause 'المشتملة على الأبحاث الجزئية' indicates that universal investigations contain or include partial investigations. So universal are general rules, partial are specific cases.
3
Examine the evaluative phrase 'من حيث إنها موجهة مقبولة'
This specifies the perspective: we consider these investigations insofar as they are directed (following proper procedure) and accepted (valid/persuasive). The opposite is also considered: 'أو غير موجهة ولا مقبولة'.
4
Contrast the two pairs to grasp the art's focus
The art is about the condition of being directed and accepted, not about the content of the investigations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وموضوع هذا الفن الأبحاث الكلية المشتملة على الأبحاث الجزئية من حيث إنها موجهة مقبولة، أو غير موجهة ولا مقبولة .
The subject of this art is the universal investigations that include the partial investigations, insofar as they are directed and accepted, or not directed and not accepted.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of the discipline known as Adab al-Bahth (the conduct of inquiry). The author states that the subject is universal investigations—meaning general logical principles—that encompass partial investigations (specific applications). The focus is on whether these investigations are 'directed and accepted' (muwajjaha maqbula) or not. The term 'directed' refers to arguments that follow proper procedural rules, while 'accepted' indicates validity or persuasiveness. Thus, the art is not about the content of particular arguments but about the formal conditions that make them acceptable in a debate.

Key Terms
الأبحاث الكلية
الأبحاث الجزئية
موجهة مقبولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The study of syllogisms only
D) The history of debate

Key Takeaway
Adab al-Bahth is the study of the universal rules that determine whether specific arguments are properly directed and accepted in debate.
