The Quranic Proof for Creation: Audience and Method
Author explains why the simple Quranic verse suffices for Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the methodological principle that determines when a simple Quranic verse suffices as a proof and when a more complex rational debate is necessary.

Core Idea
For Muslims, revelation is the ultimate proof; for non-Muslims, rational debate must supplement it.

In this methodological note, the author clarifies why he does not engage in the lengthy debates of the theologians and philosophers on the creation of the world. His audience is Muslim, and for them the Quranic statement 'Allah created everything' is a complete and convincing proof. However, he acknowledges that if the debate were with non-Muslims, a more elaborate rational argument would be needed. This lesson helps you understand the author's approach to proofs based on audience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the note
The author begins with 'Tanbih' (note). He wants to explain why he only gives examples and not a full debate. Read the first few lines to see the target audience.
2
Extract the syllogism from the verse
The author provides a syllogism: 'All of the world is created because of its inclusion in the generality of His statement...and everything created is originated; the result is: All the world is originated.' Identify the premises and conclusion.
This is a simple categorical syllogism: Major premise (implicitly from the verse) and minor premise (all created things are originated).
3
Understand the sufficiency of the verse
The author says the verse alone suffices 'without a composite proof'. Why? Because for Muslims, the verse is an accepted authority. Recognize that this is a methodological choice: using revelation as a direct proof.
4
Contrast with the non-Muslim audience
The author notes that if addressing non-Muslims, he would have to show the falsity of opposing views and use 'correct debate' combining intellect and revelation. This sets a boundary for the scope of the work.

تنبيه : اعلم أنا مثلنا للدليل على حدوث العالم بالأمثلة التي رأيت ومرادنا فهم قواعد الأدلة، مع علمنا بكثرة مناقشة الجهمية والمعتزلة ومن وافقهم من طوائف أهل الكلام، واختلاف طرقهم في إثبات حدوث العالم عقلا ومناقشة القائلين بقدم الهيولى - التي مواد الأشياء وأصولها - في حدوث العالم، وكذلك مناقشة أرسطاطاليس وأتباعه في ذلك بادعاء قدم الأفلاك، فلم نطل هنا الكلام بمناقشاتهم وبيات الباطل منها ؛ وكلامنا في هذه المذكرة مع المسلمين، وكلُّ مسلم يقنعه إقناعاً كاملاً أن تقول له : كل العالم مخلوق لدخوله في عموم قوله - تعالى - : و اللهُ خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْ [الزمر/ ٦٢]، وكل مخلوق فهو حادث، ينتج : كل العالم حادث، مع أن قوله - تعالى - : اللَّهُ خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ يغني عن دليل مركب يدل على ذلك، فلو اقتضت الحال أن يكون كلامنا مع غير المسلمين لاضطررنا إلى بيان فساد الفاسد من تلك المناظرات وتصحيح الصحيح منها عن طريق المناظرة الصحيحة، المستند فيها العقل إلى الوحي الصحيح، ولا يتسع هذا المقام لذلك، ونرجو الله التوفيق للصواب، وحسبنا ونعم الوكيل .
Note: Know that we have presented examples for the proof of the creation of the world as you have seen, and our intent is the understanding of the rules of proofs, despite our knowledge of the extensive debates of the Jahmiyya, Mu'tazila, and those who agree with them among the sects of the theologians, and the differences in their methods in proving the creation of the world rationally, and the debate with those who claim the pre-eternity of hyle—which are the materials and origins of things—regarding the creation of the world, and likewise the debate with Aristotle and his followers in that by their claim of the pre-eternity of the celestial spheres. So we have not prolonged here the discussion of their debates and the exposure of falsehood among them; and our speech in this memorandum is with Muslims, and every Muslim is fully convinced if you say to him: All of the world is created because of its inclusion in the generality of His statement—Exalted is He—'And Allah created everything' [Az-Zumar 62], and everything created is originated (hadith); the result is: All the world is originated. Moreover, His statement—Exalted is He—'Allah created everything' suffices without a composite proof for that. And if the situation required that our speech be with non-Muslims, then we would be compelled to explain the corruption of the corrupt among those debates and to correct the correct ones by way of correct debate, in which the intellect relies on sound revelation. But this place does not accommodate that. And we ask Allah for guidance to what is correct, and sufficient for us is He, and the best of trustees.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note (tanbih) clarifying the author's approach in presenting proofs for the creation of the world. The author acknowledges that he has given examples and is aware of the extensive debates among various theological and philosophical groups (Jahmiyya, Mu'tazila, hyle proponents, Aristotle, etc.). However, he explains that in this memorandum, his audience is Muslims. For Muslims, the simple Quranic verse 'Allah created everything' is sufficient as a complete proof, because it is universally accepted by Muslims and yields a straightforward syllogism: Everything that is created is originated (hadith), and the world is included in the generality of 'everything', hence the world is originated. The author emphasizes that this verse even eliminates the need for a composite proof (dalil murakkab). If the audience were non-Muslims, a more detailed and rational debate would be necessary, but that is beyond the scope of this work. The passage sets the methodological principle that for believers, scriptural authority can be directly used as proof.

Audience-Aware Argumentation in Islamic Proofs
Author explains why he avoids lengthy refutations of non-Muslim arguments, relying on Qur'anic evidence for Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of tailoring theological proofs to audience (Muslim vs. non-Muslim) using the author's method.

Core Idea
The strongest proof for a Muslim is the Qur'an itself, not complex philosophical syllogisms.

The author of this memorandum explains why he chooses to present a simple Qur'anic proof for the temporal origination of the world rather than engaging in detailed refutations of Aristotelian or theological arguments. His reason is tied to his audience: Muslims already accept the Qur'an as authoritative. This lesson teaches you how to recognize and apply this audience-aware approach to proof selection in Islamic theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author's Stated Audience
The author explicitly says 'Our speech in this memorandum is with Muslims.' This is crucial: he is not addressing philosophers or non-Muslims in this work.
Note the context: the work is a mudhakkara (memorandum) intended for Muslim students.
2
Extract the Proof He Uses for Muslims
He gives a simple syllogism: 'The whole world is created because it falls under the generality of His statement: Allah is the Creator of everything. Everything created is temporally originated. Therefore, the whole world is temporally originated.' Note that the first premise is directly from the Qur'an.
He emphasizes that this Qur'anic verse suffices without a compound proof (dalil murakkab).
3
Compare with What He Would Do for Non-Muslims
If the audience were non-Muslims, he says he would need to engage in lengthy debates (munazara) to expose falsehood and demonstrate truth using reason and revelation together. The current context does not allow that.
This shows the author's awareness of different argumentative strategies for different audiences.
4
Understand the Principle of Proof Economy
The author chooses the most efficient and convincing proof for his audience. For Muslims, a direct Qur'anic proof is sufficient and more effective than complex syllogisms. This economizes effort and avoids confusion.
This principle is widely used in Islamic da'wah and theological education.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Clarification of the Qur'an by the Qur'an, and knowledge is with Allah - exalted is He -. A note: Know that we have given examples for the proof of the temporal origination of the world using the examples you have seen, and our intent is to understand the rules of proofs, despite our knowledge of the extensive disputation of the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila and those who agree with them from the groups of the theologians, and the diversity of their methods in establishing the temporal origination of the world intellectually, and the disputation of those who claim the pre-eternity of matter - which is the materials of things and their principles - regarding the temporal origination of the world, and likewise the disputation of Aristotle and his followers in that by claiming the pre-eternity of the spheres. So we did not prolong the discussion here by their disputations and exposing the falsehood among them. Our speech in this memorandum is with Muslims, and every Muslim is completely convinced if you say to him: The whole world is created, because it falls under the generality of His statement - exalted is He -: 'And Allah is the Creator of everything' [az-Zumar 62], and everything created is temporally originated, so it yields: The whole world is temporally originated. Commentary
This passage is a methodological note from the author. He explains that in this memorandum (mudhakkara), he has chosen to present proofs for the temporal origination (huduth) of the world using simple Qur'anic examples, even though he is aware of the extensive debates among theological groups (Jahmiyya, Mu'tazila, etc.) and philosophers (Aristotelians) who argue for pre-eternity. He deliberately avoids engaging in those detailed refutations because his intended audience is Muslims. For a Muslim, a direct Qur'anic verse, such as 'Allah is the Creator of everything,' is sufficient to conclude that the world is temporally originated (since everything created is hadith). The author emphasizes that this verse alone is enough, without needing a complex composite proof. If he were addressing non-Muslims, he would have to analyze and refute their arguments using proper dialectical method, but space does not permit. The tone is practical: the author prioritizes teaching the foundations of proofs to a Muslim audience over exhaustive polemics.

Two Foundations of the Salafi Creed
Author outlines the first two of three principles for correct creed on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two foundations of the Salafi creed on divine attributes and explain the logical relationship between tanzih and tasdiq.

Core Idea
Tanzih (transcendence) must precede and ground affirmation (tasdiq) of attributes.

This passage introduces the correct methodology for understanding Allah's attributes according to the Salaf, using the precise terminology of logicians. The author emphasizes that the creed is built on three foundations, and here he elaborates the first two: absolute transcendence (tanzih) and then affirmation of attributes (tasdiq) based on that transcendence. Understanding this sequence is crucial to avoid both anthropomorphism (tashbih) and negation (ta'til).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three foundations mentioned
The passage states that the correct creed is built on three foundations, but only the first two are elaborated here. Find the places where the author numbers them.
The third foundation is not discussed in this excerpt.
2
Define the first foundation: tanzih
The author describes tanzih as declaring Allah free from resemblance to anything in creation—essence, attributes, or actions. Note the Qur'anic verses cited as evidence.
Key verses: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11) and others.
3
Define the second foundation: tasdiq on the basis of tanzih
Affirmation of attributes (tasdiq) means believing in all attributes that Allah and His Messenger have affirmed, but only after establishing the foundation of tanzih. This prevents tashbih.
The author says affirmation is 'built upon the foundation of that transcendence'.
4
Analyze the combined verse as a model
The verse 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (42:11) perfectly combines tanzih (first part) and ithbat (second part). This is the paradigm.
The author uses this verse to illustrate the correct balance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان عقيدة السلف الصحيحة الصافية من شوائب التشبيه والتعطيل. وقد أردنا أن نوضحها هنا باختصار، بأسلوب عربي اصطلاح أهل المنطق وأهل البحث والمناظرة؛ لينفع الله بذلك من أراد هدايته من خلقه. اعلم أن المعتقد الصحيح المنجي عند الله في آيات الصفات هو ما كان عليه السلف الصالح - رضي الله عنهم -، وهو مقتضى نصوص القرآن العظيم. وهو مبني على ثلاثة أسس، كلها صرح الله به في كتابه وصرح بها رسوله الله في الأحاديث الصحيحة، ولا يصف الله أعلم بالله من الله، ولا يصف الله بعد الله أعلم بالله من رسوله الذي قال في حقه: ﴿ وَمَا يَنطِقُ عَنِ الْهَوَى إِنْ هُوَ إِلَّا وَحْيٌ يُوحَى ﴾. والأول من الأسس الثلاثة المذكورة هو تنزيه خالق السماوات والأرض - جل وعلا - من مشابهة خلقه في شيء من ذواتهم أو صفاتهم أو أفعالهم سبحانه وتعالى عن ذلك علوا كبيرا، وهذا الأساس الأعظم للعقيدة الصحيحة صرح الله به في قوله: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ﴾، وقوله: ﴿ وَلَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ كُفُوًا أَحَدٌ ﴾، وقوله: ﴿ هَلْ تَعْلَمُ لَهُ سَمِيًّا ﴾، وقوله: ﴿ فَلَا تَضْرِبُوا لِلَّهِ الْأَمْثَالَ ﴾. ومن وفقه الله لفهم هذا الأساس الأعظم ونزه خالقه عن مشابهة الخلق تنزيها تاما جازمًا به قلبه فإن قلبه يكون طاهرا من أقذار التشبيه، وتكون عقيدته مبنية على أساس صحيح، وهو تنزيه خالق السموات والأرض عن مشابهة خلقه، في ضوء قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ﴾، ونحوها من الآيات، فإذا استحكم هذا الأساس الأعظم في قلب المؤمن كان استحكامه فيه سببًا لتوفيقه للأساس الثاني من الأسس الثلاثة التي ذكرنا. ونعني بالأساس الثاني المذكور تصديق الله فيما أثنى به على نفسه، وتصديق رسول الله ﷺ فيما أثنى على ربه، والإيمان بتلك الصفات الثابتة في القرآن العظيم و [السنة] الصحيحة إيمانا مبنيا على أساس ذلك التنزيه. فهذان أساسان عظيمان: الأول تنزيه الله - تعالى - عن مشابهة خلقه، والثاني الإيمان بصفاته الثابتة في الوحي الصحيح إيمانا مبنيا على أساس ذلك التنزيه بعيدا كل البعد عن مشابهة الخلق، وكيف يخطر في ذهن المؤمن العاقل مشابهة الخلق لخالقهم؟ فالصنعة لا تشبه صانعها بحال. وهذان الأساسان أوضحهما الله في محكم كتابه إيضاحا لا يترك الحق لبسا ولا شبهة، وذلك في قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾؛ لأن قوله: ﴿ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ إثبات للصفات بعد نفي التشبيه.
A chapter on explaining the correct creed of the Salaf, pure from the impurities of anthropomorphism (tashbih) and negation (ta'til). We intended to clarify it here briefly, in the Arabic style of the terminology of the people of logic and the people of research and disputation; so that Allah may benefit thereby whom He wills to guide from His creation. Know that the correct belief that saves with Allah regarding the verses of attributes is what the righteous Salaf (may Allah be pleased with them) held, and it is the requirement of the texts of the Mighty Qur'an. It is built upon three foundations, all of which Allah declared in His book and His Messenger declared in the authentic hadiths. And no one describes Allah more knowledgeable of Allah than Allah, and after Allah no one describes Allah more knowledgeable of Allah than His Messenger, about whom He said: 'Nor does he speak from [his own] inclination. It is not but a revelation revealed.' The first of the three mentioned foundations is the transcendence (tanzih) of the Creator of the heavens and the earth (Exalted and Glorified) from resembling His creation in anything of their essences, attributes, or actions - glory be to Him and exalted above that, a great sublimity. This is the greatest foundation of the correct creed, which Allah declared in His saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him', and His saying: 'Nor is there to Him any equivalent', and His saying: 'Do you know of any similarity to Him?', and His saying: 'So do not attribute to Allah likenesses'. Whomsoever Allah grants success to understand this greatest foundation and to declare his Creator transcendent above resembling the creation with a complete and decisive transcendence in his heart, then his heart becomes pure from the filth of anthropomorphism, and his creed is built upon a correct foundation, which is the transcendence of the Creator of the heavens and the earth from resembling His creation, in light of His saying - Exalted is He: 'There is nothing like unto Him', and similar verses. So when this greatest foundation becomes firmly established in the believer's heart, its firm establishment becomes a cause for his success towards the second foundation of the three we mentioned. By the second mentioned foundation we mean affirming the truth of Allah in what He praised Himself with, and affirming the truth of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in what he praised his Lord with, and believing in those attributes established in the Mighty Qur'an and the authentic Sunnah, a belief built upon the foundation of that transcendence. These are two great foundations: the first is the transcendence of Allah (Exalted is He) from resembling His creation, and the second is belief in His attributes established in the authentic revelation, a belief built upon the foundation of that transcendence, far removed from resemblance to creation. How could an intelligent believer imagine the resemblance of creation to their Creator? For the artifact does not resemble its maker in any way. These two foundations Allah clarified in His precise Book with a clarification that leaves no ambiguity or doubt regarding the truth, and that is in His saying - Exalted is He: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing'; because His saying 'And He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' is an affirmation of the attributes after the negation of anthropomorphism.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a front-matter section establishing the methodology for discussing divine attributes. The author begins by stating his intention to explain the correct creed of the Salaf (the early righteous Muslims) using the terminology of logicians and dialecticians. This indicates that the work will employ logical precision in theological matters. He then defines the correct belief regarding verses about Allah's attributes as that which the Salaf held, derived from the Qur'an and authentic hadith. The creed is built on three foundations, though only the first two are elaborated here. The first foundation is tanzih (transcendence): affirming that Allah is absolutely unlike His creation in essence, attributes, and actions. The author cites several Qur'anic verses to support this, emphasizing that this foundation must be firmly rooted in the heart, purifying it from tashbih (anthropomorphism). When this foundation is established, it leads to the second foundation: tasdiq (affirmation) of Allah's self-praise and the Prophet's praise of Him, accepting all attributes mentioned in revelation while maintaining the principle of tanzih. The author stresses that these two foundations are perfectly combined in the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (Qur'an 42:11) — the first part negates likeness, the second affirms attributes. He uses the analogy of a craftsman and his handiwork: the product never resembles its maker. This passage sets the stage for a detailed discussion of divine attributes, using logical categories to avoid both tashbih and ta'til.

عقيدة السلف
تنزيه
تشبيه
تعطيل
إيمان بالصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Affirming all attributes without any qualification
B) Negating all attributes to avoid anthropomorphism
C) Declaring Allah transcendent (tanzih) from resembling creation
D) Interpreting problematic verses metaphorically (ta'wil)

Key Takeaway
The correct creed on divine attributes requires holding tanzih (transcendence) as the foundation for affirming attributes without tashbih or ta'til.

Introduction and Front Matter

Two Foundations: Tanzih and Iman bi Sifat
Author establishes two foundational principles from Quranic verse: tanzih and affirmation of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Quranic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' wa huwa al-Sami' al-Basir' establishes both tanzih and affirmation of attributes without falling into ta'til or tashbih.

Core Idea
Tanzih and affirmation of attributes are two sides of the same coin, combined in one verse.

In this passage from Adab al-Bahth, the author lays down the two foundational principles for correct belief about Allah: tanzih (transcendence) and iman bi as-sifat (belief in attributes). He demonstrates how the Quranic verse from Surah ash-Shura (42:11) perfectly combines both, teaching us to negate any likeness to creation while affirming the attributes without analogy. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure of this argument and its application in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two foundations
Read the passage and find where the author explicitly names 'two great foundations'. What are they? The first is tanzih (declaring Allah free from resemblance to creation). The second is belief in His attributes (as established in the Qur'an and Sunnah), built upon the basis of tanzih.
Notice the order: tanzih comes first, then affirmation on that basis.
2
Locate the Quranic verse and separate its parts
The author cites the verse: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (42:11). Separate it into two parts: the first part 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' and the second part 'Wa huwa al-Sami' al-Basir'.
Each part serves a distinct purpose.
3
Understand what each part establishes
The first part establishes complete tanzih without ta'til (denial of attributes). The second part establishes affirmation of attributes without tashbih (likening to creation). The author explains that hearing and sight are qualities found in creatures, but the pairing with tanzih prevents any misunderstanding.
The verse itself teaches the correct methodology.
4
Explain how the two parts work together
The combination of the two parts shows that we must affirm attributes but without any resemblance. The author says: 'So we do not overstep before our Creator and deny Him the attribute of perfection... nor do we liken our Creator to His creation. Rather, we combine tanzih first and belief in the attributes second.'
This guards against two extremes: ta'til and tashbih.
5
Apply the framework to another attribute
Consider how you would apply the same methodology to another attribute, such as al-istiwa' (rising over the Throne) in verse 20:5. Affirm the attribute as Allah affirmed it for Himself, but negate any resemblance to creation's actions.
This is the methodology of Ahl al-Sunnah.

ومن وفقه الله لفهم هذا الأساس الأعظم ونزه خالقه عن مشابهة الخلق تنزيها تاما جازمًا به قلبه فإن قلبه يكون طاهرا من أقذار التشبيه ، وتكون عقيدته مبنية على أساس صحيح، وهو تنزيه خالق السموات والأرض عن مشابهة خلقه، في ضوء قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءُ ﴾، ونحوها من الآيات، فإذا استحكم هذا الأساس الأعظم في قلب المؤمن كان استحكامه فيه سببًا لتوفيقه للأساس الثاني من الأسس الثلاثة التي ذكرنا . ونعني بالأساس الثاني المذكور تصديق الله فيما أثنى به على نفسه، وتصديق رسول الله ﷺ فيما أثنى على ربه، والإيمان بتلك الصفات الثابتة في القرآن العظيم و السنة الصحيحة إيمانا مبنيا على أساس ذلك التنزيه . فهذان أساسان عظيمان: الأول تنزيه الله ـ تعالى ـ عن مشابهة خلقه، والثاني الإيمان بصفاته الثابتة في الوحي الصحيح إيمانا مبنيا على أساس ذلك التنزيه بعيدا كلَّ البعد عن مشابهة الخلق، وكيف يخطر في ذهن المؤمن العاقل مشابهة الخلق لخالقهم؟ فالصنعة لا تشبه صانعها بحال . وهذان الأساسان أوضحهما الله في محكم كتابه إيضاحًا لا يترك الحق لبسًا ولا شبهة، وذلك في قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى ١١] ؛ لأن قوله: ﴿وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ بعد قوله : ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ﴾ فيه سر أعظمُ وتعليم أكبر، في أوضح عبارة وأوجزها ، لا يترك في الحق لبسًا . وإيضاح ذلك أن السمع والبصر من حيث هما يتصف بهما جميع الحيوانات - ولله المثل الأعلى - فكأن الله يقول : يا عبدي، لا يخطر في عقلك أن سمعي وبصري يشابهان أسماع المخلوقين وأبصارهم حتى تقول : إن هذا النص يوهم غير اللائق فتؤوله أو تنفيه، بل أثبت لي سمعي وبصري كما أَثبتهما على نفسي، إثباتاً مبنيا على أساس التنزيه، ولاحظ في ذلك قولي قبله : ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ﴾ . وهذا تعليم قرآني لا يتركُ في الحق لبسا ولا شبهة، فأول الآية الكريمة الذي هو ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ﴾ تنزيه تام من غير تعطيل، وآخرها وهو قوله: ﴿ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ إيمان بالصفات من غير تشبيه ولا تمثيل فيجب علينا أن نعتقد ما دل عليه أولها من التنزيه وما دل عليه آخرُها من إثبات الصفات والإيمان بها على أساس ذلك التنزيه . فلا نتنطع بين يدي خالقنا وننفي عنه صفة الكمال التي أثنى بها على نفسه، ولا نشبّه خالقنا بخلقه، بل نجمع بين التنزيه أولاً، والإيمان بالصفات ثاينا، حسبما دلت عليه آية ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ﴾
And whoever Allah grants success to understand this greatest foundation and declares his Creator free from likeness to creation with a complete tanzih that his heart is firmly convinced of, then his heart will be pure from the filth of tashbih, and his creed will be built upon a correct foundation, which is the tanzih of the Creator of the heavens and the earth from resemblance to His creation, in light of His statement - Exalted is He -: 'There is nothing like unto Him', and similar verses. So when this greatest foundation becomes firmly established in the believer's heart, its firmness in him becomes a cause for his success to the second foundation from the three foundations we mentioned. And by the second foundation we mean affirming Allah in what He has praised Himself with, and affirming the Messenger of Allah ﷺ in what he praised his Lord with, and belief in those attributes established in the Great Qur'an and the authentic Sunnah, a belief built upon the basis of that tanzih. So these are two great foundations: the first is tanzih of Allah - Exalted is He - from resemblance to His creation, and the second is belief in His attributes established in the authentic revelation, a belief built upon the basis of that tanzih, far removed from any resemblance to creation. And how could it occur in the mind of a believing, intelligent person that creation resembles their Creator? For the artifact does not resemble its maker at all. And these two foundations Allah has made clear in the decisive verses of His Book with a clarity that leaves no confusion or doubt about the truth, and that is in His statement - Exalted is He -: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' [Ash-Shura 11]; because His statement: 'And He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' after His statement: 'There is nothing like unto Him' contains a greatest secret and a greater teaching, in the clearest and most concise expression, leaving no confusion about the truth. And the explanation of that is that hearing and sight, as such, are attributes of all animals - and to Allah belongs the highest attribute - so it is as if Allah says: O My servant, let it not occur to your mind that My hearing and My sight resemble the hearing and sight of creatures, so that you say that this text suggests something inappropriate and you interpret it metaphorically or negate it. Rather, affirm for Me My hearing and My sight as I have affirmed them for Myself, an affirmation built upon the basis of tanzih, and note in that My statement before it: 'There is nothing like unto Him'. And this is a Quranic teaching that leaves no confusion or doubt about the truth. So the first part of the noble verse, which is 'There is nothing like unto Him', is complete tanzih without ta'til, and its last part, which is His statement: 'And He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing', is belief in the attributes without tashbih or tamthil. So it is incumbent upon us to believe in what its first part indicates of tanzih and what its last part indicates of affirmation of attributes and belief in them on the basis of that tanzih. So we do not overstep before our Creator and deny Him the attribute of perfection with which He has praised Himself, nor do we liken our Creator to His creation. Rather, we combine tanzih first and belief in the attributes second, as indicated by the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him'.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from Adab al-Bahth establishes two foundational principles for correct belief about Allah. The passage is definitional in nature, as it clarifies the meaning and relationship between tanzih (transcendence) and iman bi as-sifat (belief in attributes). The author begins by describing the first foundation: tanzih, which is declaring Allah free from any resemblance to His creation. This tanzih must be complete and firmly held in the heart, purifying it from the 'filth of tashbih' (anthropomorphism). The second foundation is affirming Allah's attributes as He has praised Himself and as the Prophet ﷺ praised Him, but crucially, this affirmation is built upon the basis of the first foundation. After describing these two principles, the author demonstrates how they are perfectly combined in the Quranic verse from Surah ash-Shura (42:11): 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing.' The first part ('Laysa kamithlihi shay') establishes complete tanzih without falling into ta'til (denial of attributes). The second part ('Huwa as-Sami' al-Basir') establishes affirmation of attributes (hearing and sight) without falling into tashbih. The author explains that hearing and sight are qualities found in creatures, but the verse preempts any misunderstanding by pairing the affirmation with the negation of similarity. Thus the correct methodology is to combine both: first negate any likeness, then affirm the attributes on that basis. This guards against two extremes: ta'til (rejecting attributes) and tashbih (likening them to creation). In the context of Islamic logic and dialectic, this passage sets the epistemological foundation for how one should reason about divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
The correct Islamic methodology regarding Allah's attributes is to combine complete tanzih (negation of likeness) with full affirmation of the attributes as reported, without falling into ta'til or tashbih.

Evaluating Epistemological Superiority: Safety as Criterion
Author concludes with exhortation and logical argument for Salaf's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate claims of epistemological superiority between Salaf and Khalaf using the principle that safety (salama) implies soundness in knowledge and judgment.

Core Idea
Safety in following revelation is the criterion for true knowledge and judgment.

The author concludes his work with a powerful exhortation to adhere to revelation and warns against false claims. He then presents a simple but profound logical argument: the school of the Salaf is safer (aslam), and whatever is safer is necessarily more judicious and more knowledgeable. This raises a key question: how do we evaluate competing claims of methodological superiority in the Islamic sciences?

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Find the sentence where the author states that the Salaf's way is safer, more judicious, and more knowledgeable. Note the comparative forms: aslam, ahkam, a'lam.
The claim is that the Salaf excel in all three qualities simultaneously.
2
Extract the logical structure
The author argues that if something is safer (aslam), it is necessarily more judicious (ahkam) and more knowledgeable (a'lam). This is an inference by implication: the comparative of safety entails the comparatives of judgment and knowledge.
The connection is not immediately obvious; the author assumes that safety from error is foundational to correct judgment and true knowledge.
3
Test the inference with a counterexample
Consider whether something could be safer (less risky) yet less sophisticated in reasoning. For example, a literal reading of scripture might be safer from misinterpretation, but could it be less knowledgeable? The author would argue no: true knowledge is inherently safe because it aligns with revelation.
This step helps you assess the author's assumption and understand his epistemological stance.
4
Apply to a real theological debate
Choose a specific issue (e.g., divine attributes). Compare the Salafi approach (affirm without ta'wil) and the Khalafi approach (use metaphorical interpretation). Using the author's principle, argue which is aslam and therefore ahkam and a'lam.
This practical application shows how the principle works in actual Islamic scholarly discourse.

In conclusion, we recommend to ourselves and our Muslim brothers fear of Allah - Most High -, and not to assault Allah - Most High - and His Book with false claims, and to adhere to the light of sound revelation in creed and other matters; because safety is realized in following revelation, and it is not realized in anything else: 'And my path is clear for whoever is guided, but desires have become widespread and blinded.' By what we have mentioned, you know that the school of the Salaf is safer, more judicious, and more knowledgeable. Their statement 'the school of the Salaf is safer' is an acknowledgment from them of that; because the word 'aslam' is a comparative form from safety, and whatever surpasses others and excels them in safety is more judicious than it and more knowledgeable. Thus it becomes clear that their statement 'the school of the Khalaf is more judicious and more knowledgeable' is not correct; rather, the more judicious and more knowledgeable is the safer, as is not hidden. Here ends what we intended to summarize and collect, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds. It was completed on the fourteenth day of Jumada al-Awwal of the year 1388 AH, in the city of the Prophet, and we ask Allah - Glorious and Exalted - for sincerity in all our deeds, and to protect us from corruption of intention in deeds. Indeed, He is All-Hearing, Responsive, Near. And may Allah bless and grant peace to our master Muhammad, his family, and his Companions abundantly.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the book. The author begins with an exhortation to taqwa and adherence to revelation, warning against false claims against Allah and His Book. He then quotes a line of poetry from Ibn al-Farid ('My path is clear...'). The central argument is about the superiority of the Salaf (early Muslims) over the Khalaf (later ones). The author explains that the phrase 'madhhab al-salaf aslam' uses the comparative 'aslam' (safer) which implies superiority in safety, and that anything superior in safety is necessarily superior in judgment (ahkam) and knowledge (a'lam). Therefore, the common saying that the Khalaf are more judicious and knowledgeable is false; what is safest is most judicious and knowledgeable. The passage ends with a colophon stating the completion date (14 Jumada al-Awwal 1388 AH) and a prayer for sincerity and protection from corrupt intentions.

Navigating the Foundations of Adab al-Bahth
Lists topics covered in Part One of the book on dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical progression of topics in Part One of Adab al-Bahth and use its table of contents as a navigation tool for studying Islamic dialectic.

Core Idea
The index is your roadmap to mastering the logical prerequisites of Islamic debate.

Before diving into the detailed rules of debate, Adab al-Bahth provides a carefully sequenced table of contents for Part One. This list is not merely an inventory; it reveals the logical dependencies between concepts—from the purpose of the science to the types of indication. Understanding this progression will help you anticipate the building blocks of dialectic and avoid confusion when you encounter technical terms later.

Guided Path
1
Scan the main thematic groups
Look at the first few entries: Introduction, purpose, definition, necessity of logic. These establish the 'why' and 'what' of the science. Then notice the shift to foundational epistemic concepts (tasawwur, tasdiq, daruri, nadhari). Finally, the list moves to indication (dalala) and its types. Group the topics into these three clusters.
This grouping mirrors the structure of many classical Islamic logic texts.
2
Identify the progression from general to specific
The index moves from broad motivation (purpose, definition) to specific tools (conception, assent) and then to the mechanism of indication. Notice how each later topic presupposes the earlier ones. For example, 'way of knowing theoretical conception' depends on understanding 'conception' and 'theoretical'.
3
Note the Quranic examples embedded in the structure
The index explicitly mentions two Quranic examples: the tearing of Joseph's shirt as non-verbal indication, and the verse on kalala as verbal indication. These are not random; they anchor abstract concepts in scriptural reasoning, demonstrating how logical tools apply to Islamic sources.
This shows that the science of dialectic is not divorced from revelation.
4
Use the index as a self-assessment checklist
After you study Part One, return to this index. For each entry, ask yourself: 'Can I define this term? Can I explain its role in debate?' If not, revisit that section. The index thereby becomes a tool for active recall and mastery.
This is a standard technique in traditional Islamic learning (muraja'a).

Index of Part One: Introduction, the purpose of teaching the etiquette of investigation and debate, definition of the science of investigation and debate, necessity of studying logic to understand the etiquette of investigation and debate, Muslim scholars purifying logic from philosophical impurities, ruling on engaging in the art of logic, refutation of logicians depends on understanding their terminology, benefit of logic in refuting the sophistries of theologians, Muslims' sufficiency in their religion from logic, validity of logical syllogism in itself when its conditions are observed, explanation of the certainty of rational judgments in types of logical syllogism, types of acquired knowledge, conception in terminology, perception in terminology, assent in terminology, reason for naming assent 'assent', the four conceptions on which assent depends, subject in terminology, predicate in terminology, reason for naming subject 'subject' and predicate 'predicate', necessary in terminology, theoretical in terminology, examples of necessary and theoretical conception and assent, contemplation in terminology, thought in terminology, way of knowing theoretical conception, way of knowing theoretical assent, indication and its types, indication in terminology, indication of the tearing of Joseph's shirt on the lie of his brothers, indication of the saying of the Most High: 'And he has a sister' that the kalala excludes the father, the six types of indication: 1. verbal indication by convention, 2. verbal indication by reason, 3. verbal indication by nature, 4. non-verbal indication by convention, the four understandables: a. writing.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for Part One of 'Adab Bahth', a book on the etiquette of debate and investigation in Islamic logic. It lists the topics in order, starting with the introduction and purpose of the work. Examples are given, including the indication of Joseph's shirt from the Quran and a verse about kalala (inheritance). The index shows the structured approach of the book, covering definitions, types of knowledge, and the science of indication.

The Three Types of Conventional Verbal Indication in Adab al-Bahth
List of topics and technical terms in the discipline of Adab al-Bahth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of conventional verbal indication (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and identify examples of each from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Knowing the three types of verbal indication is essential for understanding how words convey meaning in Islamic sciences.

The discipline of Adab al-Bahth begins with foundational concepts of indication (dalala). The table of contents lists the divisions of conventional verbal indication into three: congruence, inclusion, and implication. These categories are used throughout Islamic logic, law, and theology to analyze how terms refer to their meanings. In this lesson, we will unpack each type and see how they function in real Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of conventional verbal indication
From the table, find دلالة المطابقة, دلالة التضمن, دلالة الالتزام. Note their Arabic names and English equivalents.
These are the three core divisions of conventional verbal indication.
2
Define each type
Mutabaqa means the word indicates its entire meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates the whole house). Tadammun means the word indicates a part of its meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates the roof). Iltizam means the word indicates something outside its meaning but necessarily implied (e.g., 'house' indicates ownership).
These definitions are standard across the tradition.
3
Test with Islamic examples
Apply each type to a Qur'anic term. For example, the word 'Quran' indicates the entire book (mutabaqa), indicates the verses (tadammun), and indicates the miracle of inimitability (iltizam).
Choose examples from fiqh or kalam for relevance.
4
Understand the scholarly disagreement
The table notes a debate on whether tadammun and iltizam are conventional or rational. Logicians consider them conventional; rhetoricians rational; usul scholars split. This affects how one argues for implied meanings.
This disagreement appears in usul al-fiqh and logic texts.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the book 'Adab Bahth'. It lists the technical terms and classifications used in the discipline of logical dialectics (adab al-bahth). The list begins with key concepts such as 'subject' and 'predicate', then moves to necessary and theoretical knowledge, followed by indication (dalala) and its types. The six types of indication are enumerated: conventional verbal, rational verbal, natural verbal, conventional non-verbal, rational non-verbal, and natural non-verbal. The four means of understanding (al-mufhimat) are also listed: writing, contract, pointing, and gesture. The divisions of conventional verbal indication include congruence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). The passage further discusses types of necessary entailments (lawazim) and the scholarly disagreement on whether inclusion and implication indications are conventional or rational. This structural outline helps the student see the progression of topics in the book.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Three Types of Conventional Verbal Signification
Listing of the three types of conventional verbal signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of conventional verbal signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and identify them in examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A single word can convey its full meaning, a part, or a necessary concomitant.

In the study of logic and usul al-fiqh, words signify meanings in different ways. This lesson introduces the three conventional types of verbal signification: full correspondence, inclusion, and implication. Understanding these is crucial for interpreting legal texts and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the passage, list the three terms: mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam.
2
Define each type
Using the key terms and commentary, define each: mutabaqa is total correspondence, tadammun is part of meaning, iltizam is something necessarily concomitant.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
For a word like 'al-khamr' (wine), mutabaqa signifies the drink, tadammun signifies its intoxicating property (part of its meaning), iltizam signifies the prohibition (necessary concomitant).
4
Recognize simultaneous occurrence
The same word triggers all three types; they are not mutually exclusive. A single utterance can have all three significations at once.

The Disagreement on Tadammun and Iltizam
Disagreement on the conventional or rational nature of tadammun and iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the positions of logicians, rhetoricians, and legal theorists on whether tadammun and iltizam are conventional or rational.

Core Idea
The status of inclusion and implication as conventional or rational is disputed, affecting how we understand linguistic evidence.

The passage lists a disagreement about whether dalalat al-tadammun and al-iltizam are conventional (wad'iyya) or rational ('aqliyya). Logicians, rhetoricians, and legal theorists hold different views. This lesson unpacks each position.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three positions
Find in the passage: logicians say both are conventional; rhetoricians say both are rational; legal theorists say tadammun conventional and iltizam rational.
2
Understand the logicians' rationale
Logicians view the word's meaning as including parts and implications by linguistic convention, so both are wad'iyya.
3
Understand the rhetoricians' rationale
Rhetoricians consider these significations as requiring intellectual inference, thus they are 'aqliyya, not purely conventional.
4
Understand the legal theorists' compromise
Legal theorists distinguish: tadammun is a part of the meaning (conventional), but iltizam requires inference (rational).

Navigating the Table of Contents of Adab Bahth
Lists the chapter and section headings of the work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to navigate the table of contents of Adab Bahth and identify the main topics covered, including the five universals and types of definitions.

Core Idea
The fihrist is your roadmap to the logical treasures within the text.

This lesson introduces you to the structure of Adab Bahth, a classic treatise on debate and logic. By examining its table of contents, you will gain a bird's-eye view of the topics covered, from basic logical categories to the finer points of definition. This skill is essential for efficiently locating and studying specific concepts in any Islamic scholarly work.

Guided Path
1
Scan the Main Headings
Read through the list of headings from top to bottom. Notice that the work begins with basic topics like 'ارتفاع المحل' (elevation of the subject) and 'المتضائفان' (correlatives), then progresses to more advanced discussions on definitions ('أنواع المعرفات'). Identify major groups such as 'مبادىء التصورات' (principles of concepts) and the section on the five universals ('إيضاح انقسام الكليات إلى خمس').
The headings are not all of equal length; some are single terms, others are full phrases.
2
Locate Key Logical Terms
Pick out terms you recognize from earlier studies: 'الجنس' (genus), 'النوع' (species), 'الفصل' (differentia), 'الخاصة' (property). Notice how they appear in sequence, reflecting the logical order of definition. Also find 'الحد التام' (complete definition) and 'الرسم التام' (complete description) under 'أنواع المعرفات'.
These terms are foundational in Islamic logic.
3
Identify the Organizational Pattern
Observe that the work is divided into sections on conceptual fundamentals, then tools of questioning, then the five universals, and finally definitions. This progression reflects the typical order of a logic textbook: from simple concepts to complex definitions.
The table of contents itself teaches you the structure of the discipline.
4
Relate to the Overall Work
Understand that this fihrist is not just a list but a deliberate outline. Each heading corresponds to a lesson in the book. By previewing them, you prepare your mind for the logical journey ahead.

Overview of Definitions and Propositions
Lists the main topics on definitions, types, conditions, and propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven types of definers and the six conditions for a valid definition as outlined in the text.

Core Idea
The outline of definitions and propositions is the backbone for mastering Islamic logic.

This lesson presents the foundational outline of the logic textbook, covering the key categories of conceptual knowledge (tasawwurat) and assent (tasdiqat). You will memorize the types of definers and the conditions that make a definition valid. This map will help you navigate the rest of the course.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main branches
The outline begins with conceptualizations (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). Note the chapter headings: 'Chapter on the aims of conceptualizations' and 'Chapter on the foundations of assents'.
2
List the seven types of definers
Find the numbered list: perfect definition, imperfect definition, perfect description, imperfect description, verbal definition, division, example. Memorize their Arabic names: الحد التام, الحد الناقص, الرسم التام, الرسم الناقص, اللفظي, القسمة, المثال.
These are the primary ways to clarify a concept.
3
Learn the six conditions for definitions
The conditions are: comprehensiveness (الاطراد/المنع), exclusivity (الانعكاس/الجمع), definer more apparent than defined, no metaphor without clue, no prior circularity (permissible simultaneous circularity), no ambiguity without clue. Understand each condition's purpose.
Al-ittiraad and al-in'ikaas are often paired as complementary conditions.
4
Preview propositional logic
The outline concludes with categorical and conditional propositions, setting up the next major topic: assent (tasdiq). Note the division of conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive.

The reality of the difference between 'speaking' (natiq) and 'laughing' (dahik). 'Nutq' in the terminology of logicians. Definition of laughter. Definition of writing. Division of genus into proximate, intermediate, and remote. Rule for proximate genus. Rule for intermediate genus. First rank. Second rank. Rule for remote genus. Definition of substance. Answer to the question 'what is it?' by definition. Chapter on the aims of conceptualizations. Rule for the definer. Types of definitions: 1. Perfect definition. 2. Imperfect definition. 3. Perfect description. 4. Imperfect description. 5. Verbal definition. 6. Division. 7. Example. Definition in language and terminology; description in language. Conditions of definitions: 1. Comprehensiveness (exclusion). 2. Exclusivity (inclusion). Clarification that 'comprehensiveness' is exclusion and 'exclusivity' is inclusion. 3. The definer being more apparent than the defined. 4. The definer not being metaphorical except with a clue. 5. Absence of prior circularity. Permissibility of simultaneous circularity. The four 'existences' according to the theologians. Inclusion of rulings in definitions. 6. Absence of ambiguity except with a clue. The reason why 'inclusion' and 'exclusion' are conditions for definitions while they are part of the definition. Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a structured outline of the book's early chapters, focusing on conceptual knowledge (tasawwurat) and assent (tasdiqat). It begins by contrasting the definitions of 'speaking' (natiq) and 'laughing' (dahik) as differentiae, then explains the term 'nutq' in logic. It proceeds to define laughter and writing as examples, then introduces the division of genus into three levels: proximate, intermediate, and remote, with rules for each. The outline then covers the definition of substance and how to answer 'what is it?' (ma) by definition. The main chapter 'On the aims of conceptualizations' (Fasl fi Maqasid al-Tasawwurat) lists the rule for the definer (dabit al-mu'arraf) and enumerates seven types of definers: perfect and imperfect definition, perfect and imperfect description, verbal definition, division, and example. It then discusses the linguistic and terminological meanings of 'definition' (hadd) and 'description' (rasm). Next, it lists six conditions for definitions: (1) comprehensiveness (al-ittiraad) meaning exclusion (al-man'), (2) exclusivity (al-in'ikaas) meaning inclusion (al-jam'), with clarification that these two terms are synonymous pairs, (3) the definer must be more apparent than the defined, (4) the definer should not be metaphorical without a clue, (5) absence of prior circularity, with a note on permissible simultaneous circularity, (6) absence of ambiguity without a clue. It also notes the inclusion of rulings in definitions and the four 'existences' (akwan) from kalam. Finally, the outline introduces the foundations of assents (mabadi' al-tasdiqat) with the rule for categorical and conditional propositions, and the division of conditionals into conjunctive (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil). This outline serves as a map for the student to memorize the logical progression of concepts.

Introduction and Front Matter

Conditions of Definitions in Adab Bahth
Conditions of definitions from the outline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a definition satisfies the six conditions listed in Adab Bahth: comprehensiveness, exclusiveness, clarity, non-metaphorical usage, non-circularity, and unambiguity.

Core Idea
A sound definition is a tool for exact knowledge; its conditions ensure it excludes nothing, includes nothing extra, and is clear.

The outline of Adab Bahth lists six conditions for definitions (shurut al-mu'arrafat). These conditions guarantee that a definition is precise and useful for distinguishing the defined from everything else. In this lesson, we unpack each condition and learn how to apply them to real definitions from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in a given definition. For example, in 'Salah is a set of specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim', the definiendum is 'salah'.
2
Check comprehensiveness (al-ittird)
Does the definition include all instances? This condition (also called al-man', exclusion) ensures that nothing that should be included is left out. If a definition of 'wudu' excludes the intention, it fails comprehensiveness.
The outline clarifies that al-ittird is al-man' (exclusion of the defined from the definer).
3
Check exclusiveness (al-in'ikas)
Does the definition exclude everything that is not the defined? This condition (al-jam', inclusion) ensures that only the defined falls under the definer. For example, if 'wudu' is defined as 'washing the limbs', it may include wiping over socks, which is not wudu.
The outline states al-in'ikas is al-jam' (inclusion of the definer in the defined).
4
Verify clarity and literalness
The definer must be more apparent than the defined, and should not be metaphorical without a contextual indicator. A definition using a rare word for a common term would fail clarity.
5
Test for circularity
Priority circularity (al-dawr al-sabqi) is prohibited, but simultaneous circularity (al-dawr al-ma'i) is allowed. For example, defining 'a' using 'b' and 'b' using 'a' in a direct order is circular; but if both definitions are simultaneous and interdependent, it is permitted.
The outline explicitly allows 'جواز الدور المعي' (permissibility of simultaneous circularity).

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents or outline from the latter part of the logic work 'Adab Bahth'. It lists the topics covered in a structured manner, beginning with 'Division' (al-qismah) and 'Example' (al-mithal), which are likely tools used in definition. Then it moves to definition terminology: 'al-hadd' (definition) and 'al-rasm' (description) both in language and technical terms. The next major section lists the conditions of definitions (shurut al-mu'arrafat), including comprehensiveness (al-ittird) which is exclusion (al-man'), exclusiveness (al-in'ikas) which is inclusion (al-jam'), clarity of the definer, avoidance of metaphor without context, prevention of circularity (al-dawr al-sabqi) but allowance of simultaneous circularity (al-dawr al-ma'i), the four concepts (al-akwan al-arba'a) from theology, inclusion of rulings in definitions, and avoidance of ambiguity. There is also a note on why inclusion and exclusion are conditions despite being part of definition. Then comes a chapter on principles of assent (mabadi' al-tasdiqat), which covers propositions: rule of categorical proposition, rule of conditional proposition, division of conditional into connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). Connected conditionals are further divided into implicative (luzumiyya) and coincidental (ittifaqiyya), with detailed discussion on the difference, rule of coincidental, and a critical note on al-Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan. Notifications include the truth-conditions of connected conditionals, naming of antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali), and the rhetoricians' acceptance of some connected conditionals. The topic of hyperbole (mubalagha) is introduced with its types: tabligh, ighraq, and ghuluw. Disjunctive conditionals are then examined with types: mutually exclusive and exhaustive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), mutually exclusive but allowing neither (mani'at al-jam' al-mujawwiza li al-khuluw), and exhaustive but allowing both (mani'at al-khuluw al-mujawwiza li al-jam'). Finally, the outline mentions a chapter on dividing propositions by quantity and quality (al-kamm wa al-kayf). This outline provides a roadmap for the logical discussions in the book, though it lacks the detailed arguments themselves.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
12 min
Types of Propositions in Adab Bahth
Outline of proposition types from Adab Bahth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given proposition as categorical or conditional, and further classify conditional propositions into connected or disjunctive, with their subdivisions.

Core Idea
Propositions are the building blocks of assent; knowing their types and structures is key to evaluating arguments.

The outline of Adab Bahth moves from definitions to propositions (al-qadaya). It presents the rule of categorical and conditional propositions, then divides conditionals into connected and disjunctive, with further subdivisions. This lesson helps you navigate the taxonomy of propositions as laid out in Adab Bahth and apply it to examples from Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is a simple assertion of a predicate to a subject (categorical) or includes a condition (conditional). For example, 'The world is created' is categorical; 'If the world is created, then it has a creator' is conditional.
2
If categorical, note its structure
For categorical propositions, identify the subject, predicate, and copula. The outline only gives a 'rule' (dabit) but does not elaborate; focus on distinguishing from conditionals.
3
If conditional, determine if connected or disjunctive
Conditionals are either connected (muttasila) expressing a relation of implication (if…then), or disjunctive (munfasila) expressing a choice (either…or). Example: 'If the sun rises, it is day' is connected; 'Either it is night or day' is disjunctive.
4
Subdivide connected conditionals
Connected conditionals are further divided into implicative (luzumiyya), where the antecedent logically implies the consequent, and coincidental (ittifaqiyya), where the connection is accidental. For example, 'If a person is asleep, then he breathes' is implicative; 'If a person is asleep, then the sun is shining' is coincidental.
The outline warns about a common error by al-Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan regarding the real condition in coincidental conditionals.
5
Subdivide disjunctive conditionals
Disjunctive conditionals are of three types: (1) mutually exclusive and exhaustive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), (2) mutually exclusive but allowing neither (mani'at al-jam' al-mujawwiza li al-khuluw), and (3) exhaustive but allowing both (mani'at al-khuluw al-mujawwiza li al-jam'). Example: 'The number is either even or odd' is type (1).

Mapping the Sections on Propositions and Immediate Inferences
Lists section headings from quality/quantity of propositions through contradiction and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the main sections on propositions and immediate inferences and state the nine conditions for contradiction.

Core Idea
A chapter outline is your road map to logical classification.

This passage is not the full lesson content but a table of contents (fihris) that outlines the forthcoming topics on propositions and immediate inferences. It lists key sections: quality/quantity, categorical propositions, contradiction with nine conditions, and conversion. Understanding this structure will help you navigate the detailed discussions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main parts
The outline is divided into sections on propositions (al-ḥamliyya), contradiction (al-tanāquḍ), and conversion (al-ʿaks). Notice the logical progression from defining terms to applying them.
Each part builds on the previous one.
2
Examine the proposition section
Under propositions, note the sub-topics: quality (kayf), quantifier (sūr), divisions of categorical propositions, and important notifications (tanbīhāt). These define the basic building blocks.
The notifications clarify how indefinite and natural propositions are treated.
3
Memorize the nine conditions for contradiction
The outline lists nine matters that must be unified for genuine contradiction: subject, predicate, time, place, act/potency, whole/part, relation, condition, and positiveness/negation. These conditions ensure that contradiction is real, not apparent.
Two practical reminders follow, addressing common mistakes in uṣūl al-fiqh.
4
Understand the conversion section
Conversion (al-ʿaks) has its own chapter with simple conversion, applications in categoricals and conditionals, and proofs of its truth. This is a key immediate inference tool.
Note that conversion does not hold for disjunctive conditionals.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents (fihris) for a section of a work on logic, likely from a textbook on Adab al-Bahth wa al-Munazara (the art of discussion and debate). It lists the order of topics to be covered, beginning with terminology for quality (kayf) and quantifier (sur) in propositions, then discussing categorical propositions (hamliyya) and their divisions by subject universality/particularity. It includes important notifications (tanbihat) about indefinite propositions being treated as particular, and about the distinct meanings of subject (extension) and predicate (intension). It also covers natural propositions and the application of quantifiers to conditionals. The next major section is on contradiction (tanaqud), explaining its definition, the necessity of changing the quantifier, and the nine conditions for genuine contradiction (subject, predicate, time, place, etc.). Two practical notifications address mistakes in Usul al-Fiqh regarding abrogation and the relationship between particular negative and universal affirmative propositions. Then follows a chapter on conversion ('aks), distinguishing simple conversion, its application in categoricals and conditionals, and proofs of its validity. The structure shows an orderly progression from basic definitions to advanced applications, typical of a pedagogical text in Islamic logic.

The Chapter on Conversion (ʿAks): Terminology and Types
Table of contents listing chapters on temporal, spatial, and logical topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the subsections of the chapter on conversion as presented in the table of contents.

Core Idea
Conversion is a fundamental logical operation.

Under 'فصل في العكس', the table of contents lists several subheadings that introduce conversion in language and terminology, simple conversion, its application in categoricals and hypotheticals, and its proofs. This reveals the systematic approach to teaching conversion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic headings under 'فصل في العكس'
Scan from 'العكس في اللغة والاصطلاح' to 'عكس النقيض المخالف'. Note the numbering and structure.
2
Distinguish between simple conversion and contrapositive conversion
Identify 'العكس المستوي' (simple conversion) as the first type, and 'عكس النقيض الموافق' and 'عكس النقيض المخالف' as types of contrapositive conversion.
The TOC shows a clear division between these.
3
Note where conversion is applied
Look for 'العكس في الحمليات' (categoricals), 'العكس في الشرطيات المتصلة' (hypothetical conjunctives), and 'انتفاء العكس في الشرطيات المنفصلة' (non-existence in disjunctives).
4
Identify the proofs listed
The heading 'أدلة صدق العكس' and 'دليل الافتراض' show that the author provides a proof for conversion.

The Syllogism (Qiyas): Definition and Structure
Table of contents listing chapters on temporal, spatial, and logical topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of qiyas as listed in the table of contents: minor, middle, major terms, premises, figures, and moods.

Core Idea
Qiyas is the heart of logic.

The final part of the table of contents is dedicated to qiyas, starting with its definition in logicians' terminology, then its division into conjoined and exceptional, and then the parts: terms, premises, figures, moods, and the conditions for productivity in the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Locate the qiyas section
Find 'القياس في اصطلاح المنطقيين' and read the subheadings that follow.
2
Identify the three terms
Look for 'الحد الأصغر والوسط والأكبر' (minor, middle, major terms). These are the building blocks of the syllogism.
3
Identify the two premises
The headings 'المقدمة الصغرى والمقدمة الكبرى' indicate the minor and major premises.
4
Note the figures and moods
Scan for 'أشكال القياس' (figures) and 'ضروب القياس' (moods). Then see 'شرطا الإنتاج' (conditions of production) and the first two moods in the first figure.
5
Recognize the overall structure
The TOC shows a clear pedagogical order: definition → division → parts (terms, premises) → figures and moods → conditions for valid syllogisms.

Navigating the Second Volume of Adab al-Bahth
Table of contents for the second volume on debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate any topic in Volume 2 of Adab al-Bahth using the table of contents and identify its logical significance.

Core Idea
The table of contents is a map for mastering the art of debate.

Before diving into the deep waters of debate and division, a wise student first surveys the terrain. The table of contents (fahras) of the second volume is not just a list—it reveals the author's logical architecture. This lesson will show you how to use it as a navigational tool and understand why each topic is placed where it is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major sections
Look at the headings: 'al-bahth wa al-munazara' (discussion and debate) and 'al-taqsim' (division) are the two main pillars. Notice that the volume begins with a recap of disjunctive syllogisms from the first volume, showing continuity.
2
Note page numbers as logical indicators
Page numbers reveal progression: definitions (p.139) come first, then rulings (p.140), then the subject (p.140) and division of speech (p.142). This order builds toward the core chapter on division (p.146).
3
Observe the treatment of division types
The author lists six types of division (real, considerational, rational, inductive, artifactual, certain) and includes a critique of a theological position (p.151) and a Quranic example (p.152). This shows division is a tool for both analysis and polemic.
4
Relate to the first volume
The recap of disjunctive syllogisms (p.396) reminds you that debate rules build on earlier logical foundations. Understanding that connection helps you see the volume as part of a larger system.

التقسيم القطعي (ص ١٥٤)
The exceptional disjunctive syllogism and its divisions. The real disjunctive and exceptional and its modes. 1 - Composed of the preventing of conjunction that permits emptiness. 2 - Composed of the preventing of emptiness that permits conjunction. 3 - Composed of the preventing of conjunction and emptiness. Conclusion of the logical premise.

Subject: Index of the second part. Discussion and debate in language and terminology (p. 139). Ruling of debate (p. 140). Subject of the art of debate (p. 140). Chapter on the division of speech into single and compound and explanation of that in which debate occurs from them (p. 142). Author's correction of the occurrence of debate in the constructional compound transmitted from others (p. 142). Occurrence of debate in definitions and divisions and its aspect (p. 143). That the enumeration in division is certain sometimes and probable sometimes (p. 153). Note: the origin in rational and inductive division (p. 154). Artifactual division (p. 154). Certain division (p. 154).
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents (fahras) for the second volume (al-juz' al-thani) of the work on Adab al-Bahth (Etiquette of Discussion and Debate). It begins with a recap of the previous volume's last topics: the exceptional disjunctive syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i al-infisali) and its divisions, including the three types of disjunctive propositions: (1) composé of 'preventing conjunction that permits emptiness' (mani'at al-jam' al-mujawwiza li-l-khuluw), (2) composé of 'preventing emptiness that permits conjunction' (mani'at al-khuluw al-mujawwiza li-l-jam'), and (3) composé of 'preventing both conjunction and emptiness' (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw). Then it concludes the logical premise (khātimat al-muqaddima al-mantiqiyya).

The main index of the second volume then follows, listing topics from page 139 to 154. The core subject is al-bahth wa al-munazara (discussion and debate). The author first defines debate linguistically and terminologically (p. 139), then discusses its ruling (hukm, p. 140) and its subject matter (mawdu', p. 140). A chapter (fasl) on dividing speech into single (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) explains which parts of speech are suitable for debate (p. 142). The author then corrects a view about debate occurring in 'constructional compound' (al-murakkab al-insha'i) transmitted from others (p. 142), and explains how debate applies to definitions and divisions (p. 143).

The next major section is 'Chapter on Division' (fasl fi al-taqsim, p. 146), which covers divisions of the universal into its particulars (p. 147). Then various types of division are explained: real (haqiqi, p. 147), considerational (i'tibari, p. 147), rational ('aqli, p. 151), and inductive (istiqra'i, p. 153). The author also critiques a position of some theologians (mutakallimun) regarding 'al-hal al-ma'nawiyya' (state of meaning) (p. 151) and provides an example of rational division from the Quran (p. 152). The last entries discuss whether enumeration in division is certain or probable (p. 153), the origin of rational and inductive division (p. 154), and then 'artifactual division' (al-taqsim al-ja'li) and 'certain division' (al-taqsim al-qat'i) (p. 154).

This table of contents serves as a roadmap for the student, indicating that the second volume focuses on the art of debate, its rules, and the logical tools (especially division) used in argumentation.

Key Terms
البحث والمناظرة (al-bahth wa al-munazara): Discussion and debate; the formal art of disputation according to Islamic logic. [Note: In the Adab al-Bahth tradition, this refers to a structured dialectical method; earlier usage in kalam was less formalized.]
التقسيم (al-taqsim): Division; the logical operation of breaking a universal into its particulars or a category into its subcategories. [Note: Stable term across the logical tradition, though types (haqiqi, i'tibari, etc.) vary among authors.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Mastering the Six Types of Division in Debate
Table of contents focused on division types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the six types of division listed in the table of contents and explain their roles in debate.

Core Idea
Division is the backbone of structured argumentation in Adab al-Bahth.

The table of contents devotes many entries to 'al-taqsim' (division). Why? Because in debate, you often need to break down a universal into its particulars or a category into its subcategories. Each type of division—real, considerational, rational, inductive, artifactual, certain—serves a different logical purpose. This lesson dissects each one and shows how they are used in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
List the six types of division
From the table, extract: real (haqiqi), considerational (i'tibari), rational ('aqli), inductive (istiqra'i), artifactual (ja'li), and certain (qat'i). Write them down with their page numbers.
2
Define each type based on the terms
Real division divides a thing into its actual parts. Considerational division divides by aspects. Rational division divides based on reason, often used in kalam. Inductive division is based on empirical survey. Artifactual division is made by convention. Certain division yields certainty.
The author notes that enumeration can be certain or probable (p.153).
3
Match each type to a typical use in debate
For example, rational division is used in theological arguments like dividing the possible from the impossible. Inductive division appears in juristic reasoning (istiqra'). Real division is used in definition. Considerational division helps in analyzing attributes.
4
Note the critique and Quranic example
On p.151, the author criticizes some theologians for their concept of 'al-hal al-ma'nawiyya' (state of meaning), showing how division can expose flawed reasoning. On p.152, the Quranic verse {أم خلقوا من غير شيء} is used as an example of rational division: either they were created from nothing or from something.

Key Terms
التقسيم (al-taqsim): Division; the logical operation of breaking a universal into its particulars or a category into its subcategories. [Note: Stable term across the logical tradition, though types (haqiqi, i'tibari, etc.) vary among authors.]
التقسيم الحقيقي (al-taqsim al-haqiqi): Real division; dividing a thing into its actual constituent parts.
التقسيم الاعتباري (al-taqsim al-i'tibari): Considerational division; dividing by aspects or attributes that are not necessarily separable in reality.
التقسيم العقلي (al-taqsim al-'aqli): Rational division; dividing based on logical possibilities or conceptual distinctions.
التقسيم الاستقرائي (al-taqsim al-istiqra'i): Inductive division; dividing based on empirical survey or enumeration of instances.
التقسيم الجعلي (al-taqsim al-ja'li): Artifactual division; division made by convention or stipulation.
التقسيم القطعي (al-taqsim al-qat'i): Certain division; division that yields certainty, not just probability.

Table of contents for Part 2 of Adab Bahth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the organization of Part Two of Adab Bahth and identify the main topics: division and definition.

Core Idea
Part Two builds the logical tools of division and definition for rigorous debate.

This lesson provides the roadmap for Part Two. As you study this table of contents, you will see how the author systematically arranges the material around the two core logical operations of division (al-taqsīm) and definition (al-ta'rīf). Understanding this structure will help you navigate the detailed rules and examples that follow.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two main sections
Scan the fihrist for the two major headings: 'Chapter on Division' and 'Chapter on Definitions'. Notice how they are separated by chapters on conditions and objections.
The fihrist is a hierarchical outline; each chapter breaks into sub-topics.
2
Examine the types of division
Identify the six types of division listed: real, considerative, rational, inductive, artificial, and definitive. Note that each has a rule (ḍābiṭ) and examples (amthila).
These types are key for analyzing how a universal is broken into its particulars.
3
Find the Quranic example
Locate the reference to the verse 'Or were they created from nothing?' which is used to illustrate rational division. This shows how division applies to theological reasoning.
Rational division is a deductive partition based on logical alternatives.
4

Notice the sub-topics on objections, answers, and the use of division in usul al-fiqh (e.g., establishing the cause, refuting heretical views). This connects theory to real debate.
Division is not just theoretical; it is a weapon in munazara.
5
Preview the definitions section
See the four types of definition (verbal, notificatory, real, nominal) and the note about terminology. This prepares you for terminological precision in arguments.
The section on definitions complements division by clarifying terms.

Navigating the Table of Contents: Division and Definition in Adab al-Bahth
Lists chapter headings for sections on division and definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main topics covered in the final sections of Adab al-Bahth and explain how division and definition serve as tools in dialectic.

Core Idea
Division and definition are the foundational tools for constructing and critiquing arguments in Islamic dialectic.

This lesson orients you to the table of contents of Adab al-Bahth, which outlines the systematic treatment of logical division (taqsīm) and definition (taʻrīf). Understanding this structure helps you navigate the text and recognize the dialectical method of exhaustive enumeration and precise clarification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two major parts
Look at the Arabic headings. The passage is divided into two main sections: one on division (تقسيم) and one on definition (تعريف). Note the sub-chapters under each.
The division section includes conditions, objections, answers, and clarifying intent.
2
Notice the sub-chapters on conditions, objections, and answers
For both division and definition, the author includes chapters on conditions for validity, aspects of objection, and answers. This reflects the dialectical structure: present a tool, critique it, and defend it.
This pattern (shurut, iʻtiraḍat, ajwiba) is standard in adab al-bahth works.
3
Find the references to Quran and usul
Under the division section, the author explicitly cites the Quranic verse 'أم خلقوا من غير شيء أم هم الخالقون' and mentions usul scholars using division to establish the cause. These are concrete examples of taqsīm as a proof.
The Quranic verse is a disjunctive argument: either created from nothing or self-creators.
4
Observe the term taḥrīr al-murād
The table of contents lists four types of clarifying intent (تحرير المراد) under both division and definition. This is a key method to avoid equivocation in debates.
Taḥrīr al-murād ensures that disputants are talking about the same concept.
