فصل في شروط صحة التقسيم شروط صحة تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه شروط صحة تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته فصل في بيان أوجه الاعتراض على التقسيم وبيان طرفي المناظرة في التقسيم . فصل في الأجوبة عن الاعتراضات الموجهة إلى التقسيم أنواع تحرير المراد ۱ - تحرير المراد من المقسَّم ۲ ـ تحرير المراد من الأقسام ١ - تحرير المراد من التقسيم ٤ ـ تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي المبني عليه التقسيم فصل في كون التقسيم دليلاً في التصورات وجزء دليل في التصديقات التقسيم في قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَلِقُونَ ) استعمال الأصوليين للتقسيم في إثبات العلة استعمال دليل التقسيم في الرد على القائلين بخلق القرآن فصل في التعريفات أقسام التعريف عند الجدليين ١ - التعريف اللفظي ٢ - التعريف التنبيهي تنبیه شرح اصطلاحات (العنوان المفهوم الماصدق ٣ ـ التعريف الحقيقي ٤ ـ التعريف الاسمي تنبيه : انقسام القضايا إلى خارجية وحقيقية والفرق بينهما وأمثلة كلّ إشارة المؤلف في أرجوزته المنطقية إلى الفرق بين القضية الخارجية والقضية الحقيقية اعتذار المؤلف عن ذكره في أرجوزته كلمات يمنعها كثير من علماء العربية من لم يشترط الجمع والمنع في التعريف اللفظي شروط حسن التعريف عند الجدليين ١ - السلامة من الأغلاط اللفظية - انتفاء اللفظ المجازي إلا مع قرينة تعين المراد ٣ - انتفاء اللفظ المشترك إلا مع قرينة تعين المراد ترجيح المؤلف كون الشرطين السابقين للصحة لا للحسن ٤ - انتفاء اللفظ الغريب والموهم . فصل في أوجه الاعتراض على التعريف الحقيقي والاسمي فصل في أجوبة صاحب التعريف أنواع تحرير المراد من التعريف ۱ - تحرير المراد من المعرَّف ضابط تحرير المراد من المعرَّف ٢ - تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف ٣ ـ تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي المبني عليه التعريف ٤ ـ تحرير المراد من نوع التعريف الاعتراض بكون التعريف غير أظهر من المعرف وجوابه الاعتراض باستلزام التعريف المحال وجوابه
Chapter on conditions for validity of division: conditions for validity of dividing the whole into its parts, conditions for validity of dividing the universal into its particulars. Chapter on stating aspects of objections to division and stating the two sides of debate in division. Chapter on answers to objections directed at division. Types of clarifying intent: 1- clarifying intent from the divided whole, 2- clarifying intent from the divisions, 3- clarifying intent from the division, 4- clarifying intent from the doctrinal foundation upon which the division is built. Chapter on division being a proof in concepts and part of a proof in assents. Division in the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'Or were they created from nothing, or are they the creators?' The use by usul scholars of division in establishing the cause. The use of the proof of division in refuting those who say the Quran is created. Chapter on definitions. Types of definition according to the dialecticians: 1- verbal definition, 2- indicative definition, a note explaining terminology (the title, the concept, the referent), 3- real definition, 4- nominal definition. A note: division of propositions into external and true, and the difference between them, and examples of each. The author's reference in his logical poem to the difference between the external proposition and the true proposition. The author's apology for mentioning in his poem words that many Arabic scholars prohibit. Those who did not require comprehensiveness and exclusivity in verbal definition. Conditions for good definition according to the dialecticians: 1- freedom from verbal errors, 2- absence of figurative wording except with a context indicating intent, 3- absence of ambiguous wording except with a context indicating intent, the author's preference that the previous two conditions are for validity not for goodness, 4- absence of unfamiliar and misleading wording. Chapter on aspects of objection to real and nominal definition. Chapter on answers of the definer. Types of clarifying intent from definition: 1- clarifying intent from the defined term, a rule for clarifying intent from the defined term, 2- clarifying intent from some parts of the definition, 3- clarifying intent from the doctrinal foundation upon which the definition is built, 4- clarifying intent from the type of definition. The objection that the definition is not more apparent than the defined and its answer. The objection that the definition entails absurdity and its answer.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for the final sections of Adab al-Bahth, covering division (taqsīm) and definition (taʻrīf). The author structures the discussion methodically: first, conditions for valid division, then objections and answers, followed by types of clarifying intent. Next, division as a proof is illustrated with Quranic verses and usul al-fiqh examples. The second half focuses on definition, classifying types (verbal, indicative, real, nominal), conditions for good definition, and objections and answers. This table of contents demonstrates the disciplined, dialectical approach of the text.

Key Terms
تقسيم
تعريف
تحرير المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the table of contents, which chapter comes first: conditions for valid definition or conditions for valid division?
A) Conditions for valid definition
B) Conditions for valid division
C) Objections to definition
D) Clarifying intent from definition

Key Takeaway
The table of contents reveals that Adab al-Bahth methodically covers division and definition, each with conditions, objections, answers, and clarifying intent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
From the table of contents, list the three main types of clarifying intent (taḥrīr al-murād) mentioned under the division section.
?
Explain why clarifying the intent of the divided whole (al-muqassam) is crucial before debating a division.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
Division as a Dialectical Proof: Examples from Kalam and Usul
Lists chapter headings for sections on division and definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how logical division functions as a proof in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, using examples from the passage.

Core Idea
Division is not just classification; it is a dialectical proof that forces the opponent to accept one of the exhaustive alternatives.

The table of contents includes a chapter on division being a proof in concepts and part of a proof in assents. This lesson unpacks how taqsīm is used as a demonstrative tool in kalam and usul al-fiqh, with specific examples mentioned: the Quranic argument about creation and the refutation of those who say the Quran is created.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about division as proof
The passage says division is a proof in concepts (tasawwurat) and part of a proof in assents (tasdiqat). This means division helps clarify concepts and provides a partial proof for propositions.
Division is not a complete syllogism but a method to enumerate possibilities.
2
Analyze the Quranic example
The verse 'أم خلقوا من غير شيء أم هم الخالقون' presents a disjunction: either they were created from nothing or they are self-creators. This division forces a choice, eliminating alternatives.
This is a classic taqsīm used in theological arguments for the existence of God.
3
Understand the usuli use
Usul scholars use division to establish the 'illa (effective cause) by dividing possible attributes and testing which one is the cause. The passage mentions this explicitly.
For example, to find why a transaction is prohibited, they divide types of transactions.
4
See the refutation of the created Quran doctrine
The passage notes that the proof of division was used to refute those who claimed the Quran is created. This likely involved dividing the Quran into being eternal or created and showing the impossibility of creation.
This is a historical theological debate.

Source Text

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

Types of Definition and Conditions for Good Definition
Lists chapter headings for sections on division and definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four types of definition according to the dialecticians and identify the conditions for a good definition.

Core Idea
Definitions in Islamic dialectic are not mere lexicography; they are strategic tools for framing debates and must satisfy precise conditions.

The table of contents outlines a detailed classification of definitions: verbal, indicative, real, and nominal. It also lists conditions for good definition, such as freedom from verbal errors and avoidance of figurative or ambiguous language. This lesson examines these categories and conditions, noting the author's preference that some conditions pertain to validity rather than goodness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of definition
Find in the Arabic the list: التعريف اللفظي, التعريف التنبيهي, التعريف الحقيقي, والتعريف الاسمي. These are the four types.
The indicative definition (التنبيهي) includes a note on terminology (العنوان، المفهوم، الماصدق).
2
Understand the conditions for good definition
The passage lists four conditions: freedom from verbal errors, absence of figurative wording (except with context), absence of ambiguous wording (except with context), and absence of unfamiliar/misleading wording.
The author notes that the second and third conditions are for validity, not just goodness.
3
Distinguish between validity and goodness
The author explicitly says he prefers that the conditions about figurative and ambiguous wording are for validity (sihha) rather than goodness (husn). This means a definition that fails these is invalid, not just less good.
This shows the precision of the dialectical tradition.
4
Examine the objection chapters
The passage includes chapters on objections to real and nominal definitions, and answers. This shows that definition is a contested tool.
Objections include the definition not being more apparent than the defined, or entailing absurdity.

?

?

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Navigating the Adab al-Bahth Manual
Lists sections and topics with page numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate and identify the major sections of a classical Adab al-Bahth manual using its table of contents.

Core Idea
The table of contents is your roadmap to the logical flow of Islamic dialectics.

Before diving into the details of propositions, definitions, and assent, it helps to see the big picture. This lesson uses the book's own table of contents to orient you to its structure. You'll learn how the topics are sequenced and why each section builds on the previous one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main parts
Scan the table of contents for the three broad topics: propositions (القضايا), definition (التعريف), and assent (التصديق). Notice that each has its own chapter heading.
The book moves from the building blocks (propositions) to definitional tools and then to epistemological classifications.
2
Note the subheadings under each part
Under propositions, look for the division into external and real propositions. Under definition, notice conditions for good definition, types of definition, and objections. Under assent, see the division into intuitive and theoretical, with further subtypes.
These subheadings indicate the key distinctions you will learn in each section.
3
Recognize key technical terms
Identify terms like خارجية, حقيقية, تعريف, تصديق, بديهي, نظري. They appear in the headings and will recur throughout the book.
Familiarity with these terms helps you quickly locate relevant discussions later.
4
Understand the logical progression
Observe that the book first deals with propositions (the material of reasoning), then with definitions (clarifying terms), and finally with assent (the act of judgment). This sequence mirrors the natural order of logical analysis.
The progression shows how logic builds from simple units to complex reasoning.

Notice: Division of propositions into external and real, and the difference between them with examples of each. 172. The author's indication in his logical poem to the difference between the external proposition and the real proposition. 173. The author's apology for mentioning in his poem words that many Arabic scholars prohibit. 174. Whoever does not require combination and prevention in verbal definition. Conditions for good definition according to dialecticians. 1 - Freedom from verbal errors. 2 - Absence of figurative wording unless with a context of clarifying the intended meaning. 3 - Absence of homonymous wording unless with a context of clarifying the intended meaning. 4 - Absence of strange and misleading wording. The author's preference that the two preceding conditions are for validity, not for goodness. Chapter on the aspects of objection to real and nominal definition. Chapter on the responses of the definer. Types of clarifying the intended meaning of the definition. 1 - Clarifying the intended meaning of the definiendum. Rule for clarifying the intended meaning of the definiendum. 2 - Clarifying the intended meaning of some parts of the definition. 3 - Clarifying the intended meaning of the scientific doctrine upon which the definition is based. 4 - Clarifying the intended meaning of the type of definition. Objection that the definition is not more evident than the definiendum and its answer. Objection that the definition entails impossibility and its answer. Objection for violation of the conditions of good definition and its answer. Non-acceptance of an objection unsupported by evidence. Chapter on naming the two parties of the disputation in definition. Chapter on assent and clarification of disputation concerning it. Division of assent into intuitive and theoretical. Division of intuitive assent into clear and hidden. Sections of clear intuitive assent: 1 - Primaries. 2 - Innate. 3 - Experimental. 4 - Common to the general public and its two types: first: sensory, second: emotive. Types of hidden intuitive: 1 - Conjectural. 2 - Mutawatir. Theoretical assent. Obstinacy. Notice about the terminology of dialecticians. Methods of disputation in assent. 400
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents of the book, listing its major sections and topics with corresponding page numbers. It begins with a notice on the division of propositions into external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya), a key distinction in logic. It then discusses conditions for good definition according to dialecticians, including freedom from verbal errors, avoidance of figurative or ambiguous language, and avoidance of strange or misleading wording. The author distinguishes between conditions for validity and conditions for goodness. The following chapters cover objections to definitions and responses, clarifying the intended meaning of definitions, and the roles of the disputants. The second half of the outline focuses on assent (tasdiq), dividing it into intuitive and theoretical, and further subdividing intuitive assent into clear and hidden types, with examples such as primaries, innate, experimental, sensory, and emotive. It also covers theoretical assent, obstinacy (mukabara), and dialectical methods. This outline provides a structured overview of the logical and dialectical content of the work.

Key Terms
قضية خارجية (qadiyya kharijiyya): An external proposition: one about actual existents, considering them as they are in the external world.
قضية حقيقية (qadiyya haqiqiyya): A real proposition: one about quiddities or essences, regardless of external existence.
تعريف (ta'rif): Definition: a statement that clarifies the meaning of a term, either by its essence (hadd) or by its accidents (rasm). [Note: In later texts, ta'rif is often distinguished from hadd and rasm.]
تصديق (tasdiq): Assent: the mental act of affirming or judging the truth of a proposition.

The table of contents reveals the logical skeleton of Adab al-Bahth: propositions lay the ground, definitions shape terms, and assent completes the act of judgment.

Navigating the Final Sections of a Dialectical Manual: Objections and Tasdiq
Lists chapter headings on objections and tasdiq in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the outline of a classical jadal text on objections and tasdiq and identify the key divisions and technical terms.

Core Idea
An outline is a map of the logical territory; it shows how objections and tasdiq are structured in debate.

This passage is not a continuous argument but a table of contents for the final sections of a book on the art of debate (jadal). It lists the topics of objections (i'tiradat) and assent (tasdiq) as they appear in dialectical practice. By studying this outline, you will gain an overview of the key concepts—from types of a priori knowledge to methods of objection—that will be elaborated later. This exercise will sharpen your ability to quickly navigate a classical text and identify its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main headings
The outline begins with 'Objection due to violation of the conditions of good definition' and then moves to 'Chapter on tasdiq'. These are the two broad sections.
2
Break down the tasdiq division
Notice that tasdiq is divided into a priori (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). Then a priori is further divided into clear (jali) and hidden (khafi). Each has subtypes.
The clear a priori includes primary, innate, experiential, and common types (sensory and intuitive).
3
Recognize key dialectical terms
Terms like sanad, man', ghasb, tanbih appear. Note their definitions as given in the key terms section.
Sanad means evidence; man' is denial; ghasb is argument theft.
4
Distinguish between types of sanad
Sanad is divided by form (limmi, qat'i, halli) and by relation to the opposite of the claim.
Limmi is causal, qat'i is certain, halli is solving.
5
Note the author's preference
The author states a preference for the sufficiency of mere denial without evidence when objecting to tasdiq devoid of evidence. This is a methodological stance.
This preference is specific to objections against unsupported claims.

الاعتراض باختلال شروط حُسن التعريف وجوابه. عدم قبول الاعتراض المجرد عن الدليل. فصل في تسمية طرفي المناظرة في التعريف. فصل في التصديق وبيان المناظرة فيه. انقسام التصديق إلى بديهي ونظري. انقسام التصديق البديهي إلى جلي وخفي. أقسام التصديق البديهي الجلي: الأولي، الفطري، التجريبي، المشترك بين عامة الناس ونوعاه: الحسي والوجداني. أنواع البديهي الخفي: الحدسي والمتواتر. التصديق النظري. المكابرة. التنبيه في اصطلاح الجدليين. طرق المناظرة في التصديق. ترجيح المؤلف كفاية المنع المجرد من السند في الاعتراض على التصديق المجرد من الدليل أو التنبيه. الغصب عند الجدليين. طرق الاعتراض على التصديق المقرون بالدليل أو التنبيه. الطريق الأولى: منع مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل وتسمياته. التعبير عن المنع بالمطالبة بالدليل. أمثلة. منع إحدى مقدمات الدليل. مثال الاعتراض على التنبيه. قلة الفائدة في التنبيه. من أجاز الغصب من الجدليين. السند في اصطلاح الجدليين. أقسام السند باعتبار صورته: السند اللمي، السند القطعي، السند الحلي. تنبیهان: التفريق بين اللمي في السند واللمي في البرهان (انقسام البرهان إلى لمي وإني). عدم لزوم صحة الدليل من صحة النتيجة. أقسام السند باعتبار نسبته إلى نقيض الدعوى.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents or outline of the final sections of the book, covering objections and tasdiq in dialectical debate. It begins with the topic of objection due to violation of the conditions of good definition and its answer, followed by the rule that an objection without evidence is not accepted. Then there are chapters on naming the two sides in a definition debate, and on tasdiq (assent) with its subdivisions. The outline clarifies that tasdiq is divided into a priori (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). The a priori is further divided into clear (jali) and hidden (khafi). The clear a priori includes primary, innate, experiential, and that which is common among people (like sensory and intuitive). The hidden a priori includes instinctive (hads) and mutawatir. Theoretical tasdiq is then mentioned, followed by sophistry (mukabara) and the technical term tanbih as used by dialecticians. Methods of debate in tasdiq are listed. The author then expresses a preference for the sufficiency of mere denial without evidence when objecting to a tasdiq that is itself devoid of evidence or tanbih. The concept of ghasb (usurpation) among dialecticians is introduced. Then methods of objecting to tasdiq that is accompanied by evidence or tanbih are given, the first of which is denying a specific premise of the evidence. This is expressed as demanding evidence. Examples follow, including denying a premise and an example of objection to tanbih, noting the scarcity of benefit in tanbih. Those who allowed ghasb among dialecticians are mentioned. The term sanad is defined in dialectical terminology, and its divisions according to form: limmi (causal), qat'i (certain), and halli (solving?). Two alerts are given: the distinction between limmi in sanad and limmi in proof (where proof is divided into limmi and inni), and that the validity of the evidence does not necessarily follow from the validity of the conclusion. Finally, divisions of sanad according to its relation to the opposite of the claim are listed. This outline prepares the reader for detailed discussions of each topic, all grounded in the Islamic science of dialectic (jadal).

Overview of Adab Bahth's Structure
Lists the main topics and sub-sections of Adab Bahth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main sections and key concepts covered in the Adab Bahth table of contents.

Core Idea
Adab Bahth is a rulebook for dialectical debate, mapping out objections, bases, and responses.

Adab Bahth (The Conduct of Investigation) is a foundational text in Islamic dialectics (jadal). This table of contents serves as a roadmap, showing how the author organizes the material: from definitions of key terms like sanad (basis) and man' (objection), to methods of objecting and responding, and concluding with chapters on usurpation (ghasb) and obstinacy (mukabara). By grasping this structure, you will be able to navigate the text and understand the logical flow of formal debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's starting point
The text begins with the author's preference for a bare objection without a basis when objecting to a bare assent. Notice how the discussion moves from simple to complex.
Bare objection (man') is the simplest form of critique.
2
Locate the three divisions of sanad
Scan for the list: causal (lami), decisive (qat'i), and analytical (halli). These are the three forms a basis (sanad) can take.
Each type corresponds to a different logical function.
3
Track the sections on objection methods
Follow the sequence: first method (objecting to a specific premise), then expressing objection, examples, and the special case of usurpation (ghasb).
The chapter on usurpation appears early, reflecting its importance.
4
Observe the six relations of sanad to the contradictory of the claim
These relations (identical, equal, more specific, more general, overlapping, disparate) show how the basis can relate logically to the opponent's claim.
This is a key analytical tool for evaluating objections.
5
Find the functions of the reasoner (mu'allil)
The table lists three responses: providing evidence, invalidating the basis, and clarifying meaning. Note the special discussion on the debate about seeing God in terms of possibility.
This shows how abstract logical tools apply to theological issues.

Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the work 'Adab Bahth', which deals with dialectical debate (jadal). It outlines the main topics covered, beginning with the author's preference for the sufficiency of a bare objection (man') without a basis (sanad) when objecting to a bare assent (tasdiq) without evidence or indication. It then proceeds to discuss methods of objecting to an assent that is accompanied by evidence or indication. The first method is to object to a specific premise of the evidence, and this includes expressions such as demanding evidence. Examples are provided, such as objecting to one premise of the evidence or to an indication. The text also discusses who among dialecticians allowed usurpation (ghasb) in debate. The technical term 'sanad' (basis) is defined in the terminology of dialecticians, and its divisions are given according to form: causal (lami), decisive (qat'i), and analytical (halli). Two notes follow: distinguishing between the causal in the basis and the causal in demonstration, and that the correctness of the evidence does not necessitate the correctness of the conclusion. Then the divisions of the basis according to its relation to the contradictory of the claim (naqid al-da'wa) are listed: six cases where the basis is identical, equal, absolutely more specific, more general, more general in one respect and more specific in another, or disparate. A note states that each of these six divisions can occur on any of the three modes of basis. The next sections cover the functions of the reasoner (mu'allil) in responding to an objection, including providing evidence that produces the same claim or what is equivalent, invalidating the basis of the objection, and clarifying the intended meaning. A distinction is made between modal propositions with possibility and with absoluteness, using the example of seeing God in terms of possibility. Finally, there are chapters on usurpation (ghasb), obstinacy (mukabara), and a completion of the methods of objection. This table of contents provides a roadmap for the student, showing the logical progression from definitions, to types of objections, to responses, and to advanced topics.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Three Types of Sanad
Lists the main topics and sub-sections of Adab Bahth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the causal (lami), decisive (qat'i), and analytical (halli) bases and give an example of each from dialectical debate.

Core Idea
The basis (sanad) is the logical backbone of an objection, and its type determines the debate's direction.

In Adab Bahth, a 'sanad' is the evidence or reason offered to support an objection. The table of contents lists three types according to form: causal (lami), decisive (qat'i), and analytical (halli). Understanding these types helps you classify objections and anticipate responses. This lesson focuses on the definitions and examples of each.

Guided Path
1
Define sanad in context
Sanad here means the basis or support for an objection. It is the logical reason why the objection stands. Note the shift from hadith usage: in debate, sanad is not a chain of transmitters but a conceptual foundation.
Always check context: a term may change meaning across disciplines.
2
Learn the causal basis (al-lami)
The lamī sanad gives the cause or reason for the claim. For example, 'The world is temporary because it undergoes change.' The basis is causal because change is the reason for temporality.
This parallels the burhan limmi in logic, which demonstrates a cause.
3
Learn the decisive basis (al-qat'i)
The qat'i sanad is certain and conclusive, leaving no room for doubt. For example, a proof from a revealed text that is definitive in meaning and transmission.
This type is often used to settle debates when other bases fail.
4
Learn the analytical basis (al-halli)
The halli sanad analyzes the claim into components. For instance, breaking down 'This act is obligatory' into its essential elements and showing each element is required.
This is a weaker basis compared to the other two, as it depends on the analyst's decomposition.
5
Practice classification
Given an example objection, identify which type of sanad it uses. For instance, 'You claim fasting is obligatory, but you cannot prove it without citing a specific verse' – here the objector demands a qat'i sanad.
Try to come up with your own examples for each type.

The author's preference for the sufficiency of bare objection without basis in objecting to assent bare of evidence or indication; Usurpation among dialecticians; Methods of objecting to assent accompanied by evidence or indication; The first method: objecting to a specific premise of the premises of the evidence and its nomenclature; Expressing objection by demanding evidence; Examples; Objecting to one premise of the evidence; Example of objection to indication; The lack of benefit in indication; Who permitted usurpation among dialecticians; The basis in the terminology of dialecticians; Divisions of the basis according to its form: 1- Causal basis, 2- Decisive basis, 3- Analytical basis; Two notes: 1- Distinguishing between the causal in the basis and the causal in demonstration; Division of demonstration into (causal) and (inferential); 2- The correctness of the evidence does not necessitate the correctness of the conclusion; Divisions of the basis according to its relation to the contradictory of the claim: 1- What is such that the basis is the same as the contradictory of the claim, 2- What is such that the basis is equal to the contradictory of the claim, • What is such that the basis is absolutely more specific than the contradictory of the claim, 4 What is such that the basis is more general than the contradictory of the claim, • 5- What is such that the basis is more general than the contradictory of the claim in one respect and more specific in another respect, - What is such that the basis is disparate from the contradictory of the claim; Note: The permissibility of each of the six divisions occurring on the three modes of basis: causal, decisive, and analytical; Chapter on the responses to the objection; Functions of the reasoner in his response to the objection: 1- Providing evidence that produces the same objected claim or what is equal to it or what is absolutely more specific than it; The difference between the modal with possibility and the modal with absoluteness; The debate concerning seeing God in terms of possibility; 2- Invalidating the basis of the objection; 3- Clarifying the intended meaning of the objected claim; - Clarifying the intended meaning of the scientific doctrine upon which the objected was built; Chapter on usurpation; Chapter on obstinacy; Completion of the methods of objecting to assent accompanied by evidence or indication.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the work 'Adab Bahth', which deals with dialectical debate (jadal). It outlines the main topics covered, beginning with the author's preference for the sufficiency of a bare objection (man') without a basis (sanad) when objecting to a bare assent (tasdiq) without evidence or indication. It then proceeds to discuss methods of objecting to an assent that is accompanied by evidence or indication. The first method is to object to a specific premise of the evidence, and this includes expressions such as demanding evidence. Examples are provided, such as objecting to one premise of the evidence or to an indication. The text also discusses who among dialecticians allowed usurpation (ghasb) in debate. The technical term 'sanad' (basis) is defined in the terminology of dialecticians, and its divisions are given according to form: causal (lami), decisive (qat'i), and analytical (halli). Two notes follow: distinguishing between the causal in the basis and the causal in demonstration, and that the correctness of the evidence does not necessitate the correctness of the conclusion. Then the divisions of the basis according to its relation to the contradictory of the claim (naqid al-da'wa) are listed: six cases where the basis is identical, equal, absolutely more specific, more general, more general in one respect and more specific in another, or disparate. A note states that each of these six divisions can occur on any of the three modes of basis. The next sections cover the functions of the reasoner (mu'allil) in responding to an objection, including providing evidence that produces the same claim or what is equivalent, invalidating the basis of the objection, and clarifying the intended meaning. A distinction is made between modal propositions with possibility and with absoluteness, using the example of seeing God in terms of possibility. Finally, there are chapters on usurpation (ghasb), obstinacy (mukabara), and a completion of the methods of objection. This table of contents provides a roadmap for the student, showing the logical progression from definitions, to types of objections, to responses, and to advanced topics.

Key Terms
السند
اللمي
القطعي
الحَلّي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of sanad gives the cause or reason for the claim?
A) الحلّي
B) القطعي
C) اللمي
D) المنع

Key Takeaway
The three types of sanad—causal, decisive, and analytical—each serve a distinct logical function in dialectical objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Choose one of the three types of sanad and write a short dialectical exchange (objection and response) using that type. Use an example from Islamic law or theology.
?
Find an existing objection from a classical kalam text (e.g., on the eternity of the world) and identify which type of sanad it employs. Explain your reasoning.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Methods of Objecting to a Premise
Lists the main topics and sub-sections of Adab Bahth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first method of objecting to an assent accompanied by evidence and explain how it works with examples.

Core Idea
The most direct objection is to target a specific premise and demand evidence for it.

Adab Bahth outlines several methods for objecting to a claim that is supported by evidence. The first method, as listed in the table of contents, is to object to a specific premise of the evidence. This lesson explores that method, including how to express objection by demanding evidence, and the special case of objection to an indication (tanbih).

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The table of contents says: 'The first method: objecting to a specific premise of the premises of the evidence and its nomenclature.' This means the objector selects one premise from the evidence and denies it, or demands proof for it.
This is a targeted attack, not a wholesale rejection.
2
Learn the expression 'demanding evidence'
The objector can say 'Why should I accept this premise?' or 'Provide evidence for this premise.' This shifts the burden of proof to the claimant.
The text includes this as a way to express objection.
3
Study the example of objecting to one premise
If the evidence has two premises, the objector might deny the major premise. For instance, in the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden,' the objector could deny 'nabidh is an intoxicant.'
This is a effective strategy because if one premise falls, the conclusion may not follow.
4
Examine objection to an indication (tanbih)
An indication is a hint or clue that points to the conclusion. Objecting to it involves showing that the indication does not necessarily lead to the claimed conclusion.
The table of contents notes 'the lack of benefit in indication', implying it is a weaker form of evidence.
5
Consider the role of usurpation (ghasb)
The text mentions 'who permitted usurpation among dialecticians'. Ghasb here means an unfair move where the objector seizes the opponent's premise or claim. This is generally forbidden but some allowed it in specific contexts.
This is a controversial tactic, but understanding it helps you recognize when it is being used.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Reasoner's Functions in Responding
Lists the main topics and sub-sections of Adab Bahth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three main functions of the reasoner (mu'allil) in response to an objection and apply them to a sample debate.

Core Idea
The reasoner must either re-prove the claim, dismantle the objection's basis, or clarify the meaning of the objected term.

Once an objection is raised, the 'mu'allil' (reasoner) must provide a response. The table of contents lists three primary functions: providing evidence that yields the same claim, invalidating the basis of the objection, and clarifying the intended meaning of the objected claim or the underlying doctrine. This lesson breaks down each function and shows how they are used in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first function: provide evidence
The reasoner can produce a new argument that leads to the same objected claim, or one that is equal or more specific. This essentially sidesteps the objection by offering a fresh proof.
Note: The text mentions the difference between modal with possibility and modal with absoluteness, using the example of seeing God (ru'yat Allah) in terms of possibility.
2
Identify the second function: invalidate the basis
If the objection had a sanad (basis), the reasoner can attack that basis, showing it to be flawed—for example, that it is not decisive or that it misapplies the causal link.
This is a direct counter to the objection.
3
Identify the third function: clarify the intended meaning
Sometimes the objection arises from a misunderstanding. The reasoner can clarify what the claim actually means, or the scientific doctrine on which it is based, thereby resolving the apparent conflict.
This function often involves defining terms more precisely.
4
Apply the functions to the example of ru'yat Allah
The text specifically mentions a debate concerning seeing God in terms of possibility (imkan). The reasoner might clarify that 'seeing God' means a possible event only in the hereafter, not in this world, thus removing the objection.
This connects abstract dialectical rules to a classic theological topic.
5
Practice using all three functions
Take a simple objection: 'You claim that prayer is obligatory, but there is no explicit verse for every detail.' How would you respond using each of the three functions?
Think about what evidence you could provide, how you might invalidate the objection's basis, or what clarification you can offer.

The Six Relations of Sanad to the Contradictory
Lists the main topics and sub-sections of Adab Bahth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which of the six relations exists between a basis (sanad) and the contradictory of the claim, and explain its significance for the debate.

Core Idea
The logical relation between the basis and the opponent's contradictory claim determines the strength and form of the objection.

The table of contents lists six possible relations between a sanad (basis) and the contradictory of the claim (naqid al-da'wa). These range from identity to complete disparity. Understanding these relations helps you evaluate whether the objection truly challenges the claim. This lesson explains each relation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Define the contradictory of the claim
The naqid al-da'wa is the denial of the original claim. For example, if the claim is 'This act is obligatory', the contradictory is 'This act is not obligatory'.
The objection aims to prove that the contradictory is true.
2
Learn the six relations
The six relations are: (1) the basis is identical to the contradictory, (2) equal to it, (3) absolutely more specific, (4) more general, (5) more general in one respect and more specific in another, (6) disparate.
These come from the traditional logical relations between terms.
3
Recognize relation 1: identical
The basis says the exact same thing as the contradictory. For example, if the contradictory is 'Zakat is not obligatory on this', and the basis is 'Zakat is not obligatory on this', they are identical. This is a direct contradiction but not necessarily persuasive.
This is the strongest opposition but requires no inference.
4
Recognize relation 2: equal
The basis is logically equivalent to the contradictory but expressed differently. Example: Contradictory 'This is not a horse' and basis 'This is a non-horse animal' (if horse and non-horse animal are mutually exclusive).
Equivalence in meaning can strengthen the objection.
5
Recognize relation 3: absolutely more specific
The basis is a strict subset of the contradictory. For instance, contradictory 'This is not an animal' and basis 'This is not a horse' – if it's not a horse, it might still be another animal, so the basis does not entail the contradictory. This is a weaker objection.
This relation usually does not succeed unless additional premises are added.

The author's preference for the sufficiency of bare objection without basis in objecting to assent bare of evidence or indication; Usurpation among dialecticians; Methods of objecting to assent accompanied by evidence or indication; The first method: objecting to a specific premise of the premises of the evidence and its nomenclature; Expressing objection by demanding evidence; Examples; Objecting to one premise of the evidence; Example of objection to indication; The lack of benefit in indication; Who permitted usurpation among dialecticians; The basis in the terminology of dialecticians; Divisions of the basis according to its form: 1- Causal basis, 2- Decisive basis, 3- Analytical basis; Two notes: 1- Distinguishing between the causal in the basis and the causal in demonstration; Division of demonstration into (causal) and (inferential); 2- The correctness of the evidence does not necessitate the correctness of the conclusion; Divisions of the basis according to its relation to the contradictory of the claim: 1- What is such that the basis is the same as the contradictory of the claim, 2- What is such that the basis is equal to the contradictory of the claim, • What is such that the basis is absolutely more specific than the contradictory of the claim, 4 What is such that the basis is more general than the contradictory of the claim, • 5- What is such that the basis is more general than the contradictory of the claim in one respect and more specific in another respect, - What is such that the basis is disparate from the contradictory of the claim; The difference between the modal with possibility and the modal with absoluteness; The debate concerning seeing God in terms of possibility; 2- Invalidating the basis of the objection; 3- Clarifying the intended meaning of the objected claim; - Clarifying the intended meaning of the scientific doctrine upon which the objected was built; Chapter on usurpation; Chapter on obstinacy; Completion of the methods of objecting to assent accompanied by evidence or indication.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the work 'Adab Bahth', which deals with dialectical debate (jadal). It outlines the main topics covered, beginning with the author's preference for the sufficiency of a bare objection (man') without a basis (sanad) when objecting to a bare assent (tasdiq) without evidence or indication. It then proceeds to discuss methods of objecting to an assent that is accompanied by evidence or indication. The first method is to object to a specific premise of the evidence, and this includes expressions such as demanding evidence. Examples are provided, such as objecting to one premise of the evidence or to an indication. The text also discusses who among dialecticians allowed usurpation (ghasb) in debate. The technical term 'sanad' (basis) is defined in the terminology of dialecticians, and its divisions are given according to form: causal (lami), decisive (qat'i), and analytical (halli). Two notes follow: distinguishing between the causal in the basis and the causal in demonstration, and that the correctness of the evidence does not necessitate the correctness of the conclusion. Then the divisions of the basis according to its relation to the contradictory of the claim (naqid al-da'wa) are listed: six cases where the basis is identical, equal, absolutely more specific, more general, more general in one respect and more specific in another, or disparate. A note states that each of these six divisions can occur on any of the three modes of basis. The next sections cover the functions of the reasoner (mu'allil) in responding to an objection, including providing evidence that produces the same claim or what is equivalent, invalidating the basis of the objection, and clarifying the intended meaning. A distinction is made between modal propositions with possibility and with absoluteness, using the example of seeing God in terms of possibility. Finally, there are chapters on usurpation (ghasb), obstinacy (mukabara), and a completion of the methods of objection. This table of contents provides a roadmap for the student, showing the logical progression from definitions, to types of objections, to responses, and to advanced topics.

Key Takeaway
The six relations between the basis and the contradictory are a powerful tool for analyzing whether an objection is logically sound on its own terms.

Parsing the Table of Contents: Naqd Ijmali and Mu'arada
Table of contents for naqd ijmali and mu'arada sections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of summary refutation and counter-argument in a dialectical table of contents and explain how they relate to the overall structure of disputation.

Core Idea
A table of contents is a logical roadmap of dialectical methods.

This lesson helps you decode the table of contents of a classical munazara text. By learning to parse the headings, you will see the systematic order of refutation and counter-argument methods used in Islamic dialectic. This skill is essential for navigating any technical work in adab al-bahth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main divisions
Scan the headings for the two 'methods' (الطريق الثانية, الطريق الثالثة). Notice that they are the second and third methods of disputation.
The first method is likely covered earlier in the book.
2
Break down the second method: Naqd Ijmali
Under the second method, you see 'شاهد النقض ونوعاه' (witness of refutation and its two types). Then two specific faults: تخلف المدلول and استلزام المحال. Also, a classification of refutation into three types: حقيقي مشهور, حقيقي مكسور, شبيهي.
Each type will have its own rule and example in the main text.
3
Examine answers to refutation
A chapter on 'الأجوبة عن النقض' lists three responses: denying the completeness of evidence, denying the failure of implication, and denying that impossibility is entailed.
These are standard dialectical moves.
4
Analyze the third method: Mu'arada
Counter-argument is subdivided into types by what is proven (opposite, equivalent, more specific) and by subject (cause vs. evidence). Also types: by inversion, by similar, by other.
The prover's answers to counter-argument are also listed.
5
Identify the concluding sections
The index ends with notes on the effect (المؤثر) and its divisions, a proof of exclusivity, a reference to the philosophers' argument for the eternity of the world, and the order of disputation regarding assent.
These indicate broader philosophical and theological discussions.

The second method: Summary refutation. Witness of refutation and its two types. 1 - The signified lagging behind the evidence and its example. 2 - The evidence of the prover entailing impossibility and its example. Division of refutation into real famous, real broken, and apparent. Rule of the real famous and broken. Example of the broken rejected. Example of the broken accepted. Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents listing the sections of a work on dialectical disputation (jadal/munazara). It outlines the second method of refutation called 'summary refutation' (al-naqd al-ijmali), which involves showing that the opponent's evidence either fails to entail the claimed conclusion or leads to an impossibility. The text then divides refutation into types: real famous (haqiqi mashhur), real broken (haqiqi maksur), and apparent (shabihi), with rules and examples. Next, it presents answers to refutation, such as denying the completeness of evidence or denying entailment. The third method is counter-argument (mu'arada), which can be done by proving the opposite (naqid), its equivalent (musawi), or something more specific (akhass). Counter-argument is further divided by cause or evidence, and types include inversion (qalb), similar (mithl), and other (ghair). The prover's responses include denying premises, nullifying the counter-argument by refutation, or offering alternative proof. The author notes his preference for allowing alternative proof. A note discusses divisions of the effect (mu'aththir): by choice, nature, and cause, with a proof of exclusivity. The philosophers' argument for the eternity of the world is referenced, along with the Sunni doctrine on causality and a gloss. The author apologizes for expanding on refuting the philosophers. Finally, the order of disputation concerning assent (tasdiq) begins with inquiry about conciseness and unfamiliarity in the claim's wording, and the prover's responses. This index serves as a roadmap for the dialectical methods and topics covered in the book.

Key Terms
النقض الإجمالي (al-naqd al-ijmali): A dialectical method of summary refutation that shows the opponent's evidence fails to support the claim or entails an impossibility, without detailed analysis. [Note: In earlier works like Ibn al-Qayyim's naqd is often detailed; here it is termed 'summary' to contrast with thorough refutation.]
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Counter-argument: presenting an opposing proof to the opponent's claim, often by proving its opposite or equivalent. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, mu'arada often refers to conflict of evidences; here it is a specific dialectical move.]
تخلف المدلول عن الدليل (takhalluf al-madlul 'an al-dalil): The signified (conclusion) lagging behind the evidence (premises), i.e., the evidence does not yield the claimed conclusion.
استلزام المحال (iltizam al-muhal): The entailment of an impossibility: the opponent's evidence leads to a logical or factual absurdity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) False claim and invalid analogy
D) Contradiction with scripture and consensus

Key Takeaway
A table of contents in a dialectical work is not just an index but a logical roadmap that outlines the key methods of refutation and counter-argument.

Using the table of contents as a guide, sketch a flowchart of the refutation and counter-argument process described in this index.

Navigating the Logical Structure of Adab Bahth
Lists the topics covered in the book, from opposition to Quranic syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main sections of a classical dialectical treatise and explain the logical progression from opposition to Quranic syllogisms.

Core Idea
This table of contents is a logical roadmap: it reveals how dialectical method moves from opposition to proofs, then to debate procedure, and finally to Quranic syllogisms.

A table of contents is not just a list; it is a blueprint of the author's logical architecture. In this outline of Adab Bahth, you will see how the science of dialectic (munazara) is structured: starting with types of opposition, moving through the reasoner's responses, and culminating in the claim that the three syllogistic figures are found in the Quran. By learning to parse this outline, you gain the ability to navigate any classical logical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of opposition
The outline begins with 'Types of opposition' (أنواع المعارضة). Note the three subtypes: opposition by inversion, by equivalent, and by other. These are the main ways a debater can challenge an opponent's proof.
In later chapters, each type is explained with examples.
2
Trace the reasoner's responses
After opposition, the outline lists the reasoner's (المعلل) answers: denying premises, invalidating by counterexample, or providing another proof. The author gives a preference for a new proof. This shows the dialectical move from attack to defense.
The note on effective cause (المؤثر) interrupts this flow but relates to causation in proofs.
3
Examine the division of effective cause
The effective cause is divided into three: by choice, by nature, and by cause. This is a logical classification used in arguments about causation, often in kalam.
The philosophers' stance on the world's eternity and the Sunni response are briefly mentioned before moving on.
4
Parse the ordering of debate in assent
The outline details a two-step process: first, inquiry about summary and unfamiliarity in the claim; second, consideration of whether the assenter is a transmitter or not. This shows the procedural structure of a dialectical exchange.
Subsequent chapters cover transmission, expression, begging the question, obstinacy, disputation, and etiquette.
5
Identify the Quranic syllogism claim
The final chapter claims that the three figures of the syllogism exist in the Quran, with the first figure exemplified by the verse 'Indeed, Allah brings the sun from the east'. This ties the entire book to Islamic scripture.
This is a common theme in Islamic logic: proving that rational methods are rooted in revelation.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the book Adab Bahth, which is a work on the dialectical method (adab al-bahth wa-al-munazara). The outline begins with types of opposition: inversion, equivalent, and other. It then covers the reasoner's responses to opposition, including denying premises, invalidating by counterexample, or providing another proof. The author states a preference for allowing a new proof. Next is a note on divisions of the effective cause (al-mu'aththir): by choice, by nature, and by cause. A proof of enumeration into three is mentioned. The philosophers' claim of the world's eternity and the Sunni response are listed, followed by an apology for expanding on refuting that claim. The chapter on ordering debate in assent details inquiry about summary and unfamiliarity, the reasoner's answers (denying multiple probabilities or giving preponderance), and consideration of whether the holder of assent is a transmitter or not. Subsequent chapters cover transmission, expression, begging the question, obstinacy, disputation, dialectical answer, end of debaters, and etiquette. Finally, a chapter claims that the three syllogistic figures exist in the Quran, with the first figure exemplified by the verse 'Indeed, Allah brings the sun from the east'. The outline provides a structured view of the book's logical progression in teaching dialectical reasoning.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Four Criticisms (Qawadih) in Usul al-Fiqh
Author lists the types of criticisms in usul: refutation, breaking, prevention, and counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four main types of criticisms (qawadih) in Usul al-Fiqh: al-naqd, al-kasr, al-man', and al-mu'arada.

Core Idea
The four qawadih are the tools for testing the validity of reasoning by 'illa in Islamic jurisprudence.

When jurists reason from a cause ('illa) to a ruling (hukm), opponents may attack the reasoning. The tradition of Usul al-Fiqh has classified these attacks into four major types: refutation (naqd), breaking (kasr), prevention (man'), and counterargument (mu'arada). Each has its own rules and responses. This lesson introduces each form and its subcategories as outlined by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Look at the Arabic structure. The author lists four headings: النقض (al-naqd), الكسر (al-kasr), المنع (al-man'), and المعارضة (al-mu'arada). These are the four Qawadih.
2
Understand al-Naqd (refutation)
Naqd occurs when the ruling is absent from its claimed cause. It has three subtypes: due to a mukhassis (specifier), a mani' (preventer), or loss of a shart (condition). Examples are given from fiqh (e.g., concession in 'araya sales). Then five ways to answer a naqd are listed.
The author notes some consider naqd a specifier, not a true criticism.
3
Understand al-Kasr (breaking)
Kasr involves attacking the wisdom (hikma) behind the 'illa or invalidating part of the reason itself. The author discusses whether absence of hikma is a valid criticism and indicates his preference that it is not.
In jadal, this is similar to 'broken naqd'.
4
Understand al-Man' (prevention)
Man' denies one of the premises: (1) the original ruling, (2) the claimed cause in the original, (3) that the description is the cause, or (4) the presence of the cause in the branch. The author states the arguer is not cut off merely by denial of the original ruling.
5
Understand al-Mu'arada (counterargument)
Mu'arada, also called qalb (reversal), presents a rival 'illa that leads to a different hukm. It is the fourth and final criticism listed.

Source Text

الكسر بإبطال المعترض جزءًا من المعنى المعلل به

الثالث : المعارضة (القلب)
The Criticisms in Usul
First: The Refutation
Definition of the Usuli Refutation and its Types:
1 - The ruling being absent from its cause due to the existence of a specification and its examples
First example: The concession in the sale of 'araya
Second example: Requiring the blood-money for accidental killing from the 'aqila Third example: Requiring a sa' of dates in the milk of the musarra
2 - The ruling being absent from its cause due to the existence of a preventing factor or loss of a condition
Example of the existence of a preventing factor: Paternity in retaliation, and deception in the enslavement of the child of a female slave.
Example of loss of condition: Absence of the nisab and safe-keeping in cutting the thief's hand, and ihsan in stoning
3 - The ruling being absent from its cause without reason
No need to answer the refutation for those who make it a specification not a criticism.
Ways of answering the refutation for those who make it a criticism of the cause
1 - Denying the existence of the cause in the instance of refutation
2 - Denying the absence of the ruling from the cause
3 - Showing the existence of a preventing factor affecting the cause or loss of a condition
4 - The instance mentioned in the refutation being exempted by a text
5 - Counterbalancing the benefit in the cause with a more preponderant or equal harm
Section on Breaking
Note: The broken refutation according to the dialecticians resembles the criticism of breaking according to the usulis
Definition of the criticism of breaking according to the usulis
Wisdom in the terminology of the usulis
Disagreement regarding the criticism by the wisdom being absent from the ruling
Examples of the wisdom being absent from the cause
Directing the statement that the ruling remains while its wisdom is absent
Non-absence of the ruling due to absence of wisdom in what is reasoned by indicators
Author's preference for no criticism by the absence of the ruling from its wisdom
Breaking by the opponent invalidating part of the meaning reasoned with
Author's justification for mentioning breaking after refutation
Second: Prevention (Contradiction)
Positions of the criticism of prevention according to the usulis
1 - Denying the original ruling and its example
2 - Denying the existence of what he claims as cause in the original (compound description) and its example
3 - Denying that the description is the cause for the ruling. The compound of the original
4 - Denying the existence of the description in the branch and its example
Author's preference that the arguer is not cut off merely by denial of the original ruling
Arguer's answers to the previous objections
Third: Counterargument (Reversal)
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a structural outline of the main criticisms (qawadih) discussed in Usul al-Fiqh regarding the reasoning by 'illa (cause). The author classifies them into four major categories: al-naqd (refutation), al-kasr (breaking), al-man' (prevention/contradiction), and al-mu'arada (counterargument). Each category is further subdivided. For al-naqd, the author notes that it involves the ruling (hukm) failing to accompany its claimed 'illa due to three reasons: existence of a mukhassis (specifier), existence of a mani' (preventer), or loss of a shart (condition). Examples from fiqh are given for each, such as the concession in 'araya sales, the blood-money on the 'aqila for accidental killing, and the requirement of a sa' of dates for the musarra. The author then lists five possible responses to a naqd when it is considered a valid criticism. Next, the section on al-kasr includes a note that the 'broken refutation' in jadal resembles the 'kasr' criticism in usul. The author discusses the concept of hikma (wisdom) and whether the absence of wisdom from the ruling is a valid criticism. The author indicates his preferred position—that absence of wisdom does not constitute a valid criticism. Then, al-man' is introduced with four positions: denying the original ruling, denying the claimed cause in the original, denying that the description is the cause, and denying the description in the branch. The author also states his view that the arguer is not cut off solely by denial of the original ruling. Finally, al-mu'arada is listed as the third criticism, which involves countering with a rival 'illa (reversal). This outline serves as a roadmap for the detailed discussion that follows.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): A criticism in usul where the hukm (ruling) is absent from its claimed 'illa (cause) due to a mukhassis, mani', or loss of shart. [Note: In mantiq, naqd refers to immediate inference by obversion; here it is a specific dialectical tool in usul al-fiqh.]
الكسر (al-kasr): A criticism where the 'illa is attacked by showing that its underlying hikma (wisdom) does not produce the hukm, or by invalidating part of the reason itself. [Note: In jadal, kasr is similar but often called 'broken naqd'; this usuli definition is more technical.]
المنع (al-man'): A criticism denying one of the premises of the argument: the original ruling, the existence of the description, its causality, or its presence in the branch.
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): A criticism where the opponent presents a rival 'illa that leads to a different hukm, also known as qalb (reversal).
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause or ratio legis that grounds the legal ruling; its presence entails the hukm.
الحكم (al-hukm): The legal ruling that is derived from the 'illa.
الحكمة (al-hikma): The wisdom or underlying purpose behind the 'illa; not necessarily co-extensive with the hukm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which criticism (qadih) involves the ruling failing to follow its cause because of a mukhassis (specifier)?
A) al-Kasr (breaking)
B) al-Man' (prevention)
C) al-Naqd (refutation)
D) al-Mu'arada (counterargument)

Key Takeaway
The four qawadih—naqd, kasr, man', mu'arada—are the standardized forms of attacking an 'illa-based argument in usul al-fiqh, each with specific definitions and rejoinders.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a scenario: A jurist claims that 'intoxication' is the 'illa for the prohibition of wine. An opponent says: 'But date-nabidh intoxicates and is not prohibited in all schools.' Identify which qadih the opponent is using and which subtype of naqd (if applicable) and explain.
?

Introduction and Front Matter

Al-Qawl bi'l-Mujib: A Dialectical Response in Adab al-Bahth
List of headings for the eleventh dialectical question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four aspects of al-qawl bi'l-mujib and explain how it functions as a type of opposition in jadal.

Core Idea
Al-qawl bi'l-mujib accepts the opponent's statement but neutralizes its refutation.

In dialectical debate, not every affirmation is a concession. Al-qawl bi'l-mujib (affirming by way of necessity) is a sophisticated move where the opponent accepts the proponent's wording but denies its intended force. This lesson explores the four aspects of this concept as outlined in a classical Adab al-Bahth work, and how the author distinguishes it from a mere defect in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its context
Find 'al-qawl bi'l-mujib' in the source and note its placement as the eleventh question in a series of dialectical defects.
2
List the four aspects
From the numbered list, extract the four situations where al-qawl bi'l-mujib occurs: due to a defect in the negation, upon affirmation, due to inclusiveness, and due to the proponent's silence.
3
Examine the author's correction of Fakhr al-Razi
The author corrects Razi who restricted al-qawl bi'l-mujib to a defect of the cause only. Understand why the author expands it.
4
Classify al-qawl bi'l-mujib as opposition
The author prefers to view al-qawl bi'l-mujib as a type of opposition (mu'arada) rather than a defect. Grasp the significance of this reclassification.

Source Text

Chapter on the eleventh question: affirming by way of necessity. Aspects of affirming by way of necessity: 1 - that it is presented due to a defect in the side of negation; 2 - that it occurs upon affirmation; 3 - that it occurs due to the inclusiveness of the proponent's wording of an agreed upon form; 4 - the proponent's silence about an unknown premise. The author's preference that affirming by way of necessity is a type of opposition. Chapter on the twelfth question: non-verification of the benefit. Chapter on the thirteenth question: obscurity of the description. Chapter on the fourteenth question: lack of discipline of the description. Chapter on the fifteenth question: contradiction of the ruling of the branch to the ruling of the origin. Chapter on clarifying the methods of disputation of the theologians regarding the divine attributes. Disputation of the theologians regarding al-istiwa' over the Throne. First aspect: prevention. Second aspect: corruption of the analogy. Third aspect: opposition by the like. Chapter on clarifying the creed of the Salaf regarding the attributes. The building of the Salaf's creed regarding the attributes on three foundations: 1 - declaring the Creator free from similarity to His creation; 2 - establishing the attributes mentioned in the Book and the Sunnah; 3 - cutting off hope of grasping the modality. Two warnings: First: the suspicion of asking about the modality and its answer. 1 - The statement about the attributes is like the statement about the essence. 2 - The statement about some attributes is like the statement about the rest. Second: the suspicion of necessitating anthropomorphism from the Arabic language regarding al-istiwa'. 1 - The distinction between the Creator and the creation among the Arabs. 2 - The same suspicion runs in the rest of the attributes. Conclusion on the comparison between the school of the Salaf and the school of the Khalaf. Necessary consequences of the school of the Khalaf:

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents from a work on the Adab al-Bahth (the etiquette of dialectical inquiry). It lists chapter headings from the eleventh question onward, covering key topics in jadal such as 'affirming by way of necessity' (al-qawl bi'l-mujib), which is a type of response in disputation, and various defects or objections in dialectical reasoning. The later sections move into theological disputations about the divine attributes, specifically the issue of al-istiwa' (establishing over the Throne), comparing the methodology of the Salaf (early Muslims) and the Khalaf (later theologians). The headings indicate a structured approach to evaluating arguments and defining correct creedal positions.

Introduction and Front Matter

Theological Disputation on Istiwa' and the Salaf-Khalaf Divide
Outline of theological disputation on istiwa' and comparison of Salaf and Khalaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Salaf and Khalaf methodologies on divine attributes, using the example of al-istiwa'.

Core Idea
The Salaf affirm divine attributes without modality; the Khalaf reinterpret them.

The divine attribute of al-istiwa' (establishing over the Throne) has been a central point of contention between the Salaf and the Khalaf. This lesson, based on a table of contents from an Adab al-Bahth work, outlines the disputation methods used by theologians and the three foundations of the Salaf creed. You will learn how these approaches differ and why the author warns against anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputation methods
From the headings, list the three aspects of the theologians' disputation on istiwa': prevention (man'), corruption of analogy (fasad al-i'tibar), and opposition by the like (mu'arada bi'l-mithl).
2
Describe the Salaf's three foundations
The Salaf build their creed on: declaring Allah free from similarity to creation, affirming all attributes from the Book and Sunnah, and abandoning any attempt to grasp the modality (kayfiyya).
3
Analyze the two warnings
First, the suspicion about asking 'how' is answered by comparing statements about attributes to statements about the essence. Second, the linguistic suspicion about istiwa' implying anthropomorphism is defused by noting that Arabs distinguish between Creator and creation.
4
Contrast with the Khalaf's methodology
The Khalaf (later theologians) often resort to metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid anthropomorphism. The author lists the necessary consequences of the Khalaf school, implying a critique.

Second aspect: corruption of the analogy. Third aspect: opposition by the like. Chapter on clarifying the creed of the Salaf regarding the attributes. The building of the Salaf's creed regarding the attributes on three foundations: 1 - declaring the Creator free from similarity to His creation; 2 - establishing the attributes mentioned in the Book and the Sunnah; 3 - cutting off hope of grasping the modality. Two warnings: First: the suspicion of asking about the modality and its answer. 1 - The statement about the attributes is like the statement about the essence. 2 - The statement about some attributes is like the statement about the rest. Second: the suspicion of necessitating anthropomorphism from the Arabic language regarding al-istiwa'. 1 - The distinction between the Creator and the creation among the Arabs. 2 - The same suspicion runs in the rest of the attributes. Conclusion on the comparison between the school of the Salaf and the school of the Khalaf. Necessary consequences of the school of the Khalaf:
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents from a work on the Adab al-Bahth (the etiquette of dialectical inquiry). It lists chapter headings from the eleventh question onward, covering key topics in jadal such as 'affirming by way of necessity' (al-qawl bi'l-mujib), which is a type of response in disputation, and various defects or objections in dialectical reasoning. The later sections move into theological disputations about the divine attributes, specifically the issue of al-istiwa' (establishing over the Throne), comparing the methodology of the Salaf (early Muslims) and the Khalaf (later theologians). The headings indicate a structured approach to evaluating arguments and defining correct creedal positions.

Checkpoint
What is the first foundation of the Salaf's creed regarding attributes according to the passage?
A) Establishing the attributes mentioned in the Book and Sunnah
B) Declaring the Creator free from similarity to His creation
C) Cutting off hope of grasping the modality
D) Reinterpreting istiwa' metaphorically

Key Takeaway
The Salaf and Khalaf differ fundamentally in their approach to divine attributes: the Salaf affirm without modality, while the Khalaf resort to metaphorical interpretation, leading to distinct theological consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the three foundations of the Salaf creed from the lesson, analyze the following statement: 'Allah is above the Throne in a manner befitting His majesty.' Is this consistent with the Salaf methodology? Why or why not?
?
Compare the three disputation methods (man', fasad al-i'tibar, mu'arada bi'l-mithl) with the dialectical concepts from the first lesson. How might al-qawl bi'l-mujib relate to these methods?
